# Necropolitics K

## Notes

This file was overseen by Marquis and DJ Williams, curated by Elaine,
and was contributed to by Amina, Jason, Vivian, Tiffany, Maxwell, and
Bo. Thanks to everyone in the KLAB who helped put this file together.

For any questions about this file, see <https://a.co/d/64Bhzso>.

## 1NC

### 1NC---Western Man

#### Western Man defines the telos of modernity's political orientation and its subsequent onto-epistemic registers. This overrepresentation results in a mythological capture of biocentric logics, producing the instrumentality of racialized bodies and the Earth.

**Mbembe 2023** (Achille Mbembe is a Cameroonian historian and political
theorist who is a research professor in history and politics at the Wits
Institute for Social and Economy Research at the University of the
Witwatersrand \"Death Itself\| The Politics of Human Extinction.\" Every
Living Thing: The Politics of Life in Common 2023//Jean Devon Rousseau)

I haven't the space to detail the many critiques of Arendt's central
theses in The Human Condition, particularly since her writing on
plurality is not the location of this chapter but its place of
departure. But one critique is directly related to my argument: the gaps
and frictions in her work concerning identity and difference.38 Earlier,
I suggested that plurality is not extensively theorized in The Human
Condition because it is presented as a matter of fact. Yet the "Man" at
the heart of Arendt's plurality emerges not from a womb but from a
story. As Sylvia Wynter argues in her essay "Unsettling the Coloniality
of Being/Power/ Truth/Freedom," extending a line of anticolonial
thinking with roots in Frantz Fanon and Aimé Césaire, [**"Man" is the
outcome of an effort to present a dominating subset of humanity** as
human being, the unmarked, universal center of Homo sapiens, **the
referent that depends on the exclusion of human Others for its
legibility** and existence]{.underline}.39

[**Man**, in other words, has been the **rhetorical, epistemological,
and ontological accomplishment of white, Western, Enlightenment
elites.**]{.underline} In Wynter's tracing of [Man's historical
emergence under the conditions of theology, colonialism, modernity, and
racism, she offers a powerful reminder that **appeals to species
commonality in Man's name are difficult to unravel from the discourses
of hierarchy and violent forms of domination** and exclusion that gave
him birth]{.underline}. It is a mistake to say, however, that Wynter is
opposed to speaking of humanity as such, or what she calls an
"ecumenical" humanity.40 Rather, [she is opposed to the
overrepresentation of the "West's biocentric Man" in our stories of
human origins and human futures, "the incorporation of all forms of
human being into a single homogenized descriptive statement that is
based on the figure of the **West's liberal monohumanist
Man.**"]{.underline} [The problem with this figure]{.underline},
"[ostensibly natural-scientific]{.underline}," she explains, [is that it
**presumes that the human is** "like all **purely biological species**,
a natural organism,]{.underline} . . . and therefore exists, as such, in
a relationship of pure continuity with all other living beings (rather
than in one of both continuity and discontinuity)."41

#### \[[INSERT LINK]{.mark}\]

#### We endorse bio-plurality as a response to western man colonial/racial modes of violence

**Mbembe 2023** (Achille Mbembe is a Cameroonian historian and political
theorist who is a research professor in history and politics at the Wits
Institute for Social and Economy Research at the University of the
Witwatersrand \"Death Itself\| The Politics of Human Extinction.\" Every
Living Thing: The Politics of Life in Common 2023//Jean Devon Rousseau)

**[For those of us not taught to see the other-than-human world as
kin,]{.underline}** it may be difficult to understand the complex moral
bonds and mutual responsibility that are reflected in the Gwitchin
statement. [But **if meaningful political action is to be taken** at
this critical moment, **a different way of thinking is essential.**
Species thinking is a useful tool to imagine how **human and
other-than-human lives, deaths, and futures are intertwined**, and it
serves an important rhetorical role with regard to scholarship,
advocacy, and policy.]{.underline} But species works like any
terministic screen. [By focusing tightly on similarity, species thinking
occludes the differences by which we may identify the working of power:
the fulcrum of both resistance and justice.]{.underline} In contrast,
this chapter has offered [the idea of **bioplurality**, a way of
**thinking** with, within, and **beyond species**, toggling back and
forth between individual and kind, similarity and difference,
connections and distinctions, noting resonances while also listening
carefully for moments of dissonance]{.underline}. By highlighting
similarity, bioplurality avoids the twin traps of humanist and
antihumanist exceptionalism that assume that humans are outside the
living world or that we are somehow exempt from threats to it. **[By
attending to difference, bioplurality marks humanity's unique
culpability for the current crises but also its unequal responsibility
for those crises and their unequal effects.]{.underline}**

### 1NC---Set Col

#### Settler colonialism is an ongoing structure of dispossession and genocide. Settler states sustain their sovereignty through necropolitical governance that exposes racialized populations to "death worlds" while assimilating resistance through symbolic appeals to progress to obscure the intentional architecture of violence. 

**Peacock 25** (Ethan Peacock, Honors Student Majoring in Philosophy
Concentration Law & Political Science at Georgia Southern University;
5-13-25; "Invasion as a Structure: Carceral Capitalism, Necropolitics,
and the Persistence of Settler-Colonialism", *Honors College Theses*.
1054.; https://digitalcommons.georgiasouthern.edu/honors-theses/1054) --
Langham EY

I argue that [**settler-colonialism is not a closed historical event but
a living structure**: a foundational system that continues to shape the
political, legal, and socio-economic conditions of the
contemporary.]{.underline} In the United States, settler-colonialism is
often misrepresented as a "bad chapter" of the nation's history, an
embarrassing but resolved episode overcome by liberal reform. In Canada,
reconciliation efforts have become performative gestures, symbolic
acknowledgments of past harm that leave intact the systems responsible
for ongoing dispossession and oppression. These [narratives]{.underline}
function to [obscure the reality that **settler-colonial power remains
fully operational, embedded in** **the** very **institutions**,
infrastructures, and ideologies **that organize modern
life.**]{.underline} Glen Sean Coulthard's foundational structural
critique that ["**invasion is a structure and not an event**" compels us
to recognize that settler-colonialism is not merely a historical
phenomenon that has concluded; it is an enduring structural condition
that continues to influence the political, legal, and socio-economic
realities of contemporary nation-states.]{.underline}1 I assert that
[**settler-colonialism** persists]{.underline} not only through overt or
visible forms of state violence but also through [a pervasive network of
ideological, economic, and carceral systems that marginalize, exploit,
**dehumanize**, and dispossess **racialized peoples under the guise of
democratic progress, inclusion, and security**. This paradox is
especially striking because **these practices unfold within
self-identified liberal democracies**, where ideals of equality,
justice, and pluralism are professed **even as racialized dispossession
and colonial domination are embedded into their** legal and
**institutional** **architectures.**]{.underline}

**[Settler states]{.underline}** do not **[govern]{.underline}** by
fostering life but [**by managing death and disappearance.** Their power
is enacted not only through spectacular violence but through **routine
legal, economic**, and carceral **systems** that **regulate populations
and secure settler sovereignty.** Prisons, pipelines, borders, and
militarized policing work together to structure dispossession and
marginalization as ordinary. The **rhetoric of** democracy, inclusion,
and **security masks the continued reproduction of racialized** and
colonial **hierarchies.** Settler-colonialism adapts, modernizing its
forms while preserving its core logics.]{.underline} Coulthard's concept
of [the "**politics of recognition**"]{.underline} in Canada [reveals
how **settler states** reproduce the structures of settler-colonial
power by **absorbing resistance through symbolic inclusion while
preserving territorial sovereignty.**]{.underline}2 In Discourse on
Colonialism, Aimé Césaire's analysis of [colonialism as a foundational
infrastructure of capitalism illustrates how dispossession and
racialization remain necessary for maintaining settler
hegemony.]{.underline}3 Angela Davis and Michelle Alexander illustrate
how the prison-industrial complex (PIC) and mass incarceration function
as mechanisms for a carceral arm of racial capitalism designed to
dehumanize, alienate, commodify, and render disposable racialized and
marginalized peoples.4 Achille Mbembe provides the necropolitical
framework to elucidate how [contemporary **sovereignty** is increasingly
**exercised through the strategic exposure of specific populations to
premature death, structural abandonment, and systemic
neglect.**]{.underline} By situating symbolic bureaucratic appeasement,
anti-DEI rhetoric, and the prison-industrial complex (PIC) within the
broader context of settler-colonial capitalism, this research will
demonstrate how [contemporary **governance** is not just about simply
controlling people and power but about **structuring dispossession and
systematizing manufactured precarity.**]{.underline}

This project synthesizes these frameworks with a series of interrelated
case studies: the repression of land defenders at Standing Rock, the
development of militarized police infrastructure like Cop City, the
institutional decay of Mississippi's carceral system, and the Israeli
occupation of Palestine. Each represents a different node in the global
system of settler-colonial violence, technologically sophisticated,
transnationally coordinated, and rhetorically legitimized as progress or
security. While this project draws connections across contexts such as
the United States, Canada, and Israel, it acknowledges that these states
would not self-identify as [settler-colonial regimes]{.underline};
instead, they [justify their actions through the language of national
security, democratic governance, and legal sovereignty.]{.underline}
This interpretive framework is therefore intentionally critical,
foregrounding the structural logics that persist beneath official
narratives. Across these case studies, [settler-colonial states do not
govern by securing life; they govern by manufacturing its
disappearance.]{.underline} Through prisons, pipelines, surveillance
towers, and carceral practices, I contend that [**settler states like**
**the United States**]{.underline}, Canada, and Israel [**sustain
themselves through overlapping mechanisms of symbolic recognition,
capitalist accumulation, and necropolitical governance.** These systems
do not merely fail marginalized populations; they are structured to
oppress and erase them.]{.underline} The prison-industrial complex, the
occupation of Palestine, and the institutional collapse of Parchman
Prison all demonstrate how **[states produce]{.underline}** what Mbembe
calls ["**death worlds**," **zones in which the capacity to live is
degraded by design.**]{.underline}5 [These zones are not accidental;
they are the product of intentional policies, spatial control, and
infrastructural neglect.]{.underline} They are sophisticated expressions
of settler-colonial sovereignty.

#### \[[INSERT LINK]{.mark}\]

**Becker 22** (Matilda Becker, Strategic Partnerships Manager and a
Research Fellow at Oxford Net Zero; 2022; "Arctic mineral futures:
tracing geoscience knowledge from field to community in the Northwest
Territories, Canada";
https://ora.ox.ac.uk/objects/uuid:5220d418-281b-48d7-91de-8789fc8a149f)
-- Langham EY

[This thesis frames the subterranean as a space that is still in the
making and whose constitution is not yet settled. It views the
activities and products of **geological mapping as generative processes
that enable us to imagine the subterranean**, expand down into it and
draw it up into our surface worlds.]{.underline} As I will show in this
thesis, [**geological maps of the Arctic** today, as nearly 500 years
ago, contain reflections of societal meanings and aspirations of what
could or should become of a place, and resultingly, become a mechanism
to invent and **produce**]{.underline} (**[economic]{.underline}**)
**[spaces.]{.underline}**

[The production and realisation of imagined spaces is crucial to the
curation of socio-political and socio-environmental relations with
place.]{.underline} As political ecologists have noted, [**mapping** has
worked to **rationalise colonial interventions into colonised lands**
and supported the **transformation of lands'** existing ecosystems or
communities **into supporting** more **European, 'rationalised' forms of
spatial production**]{.underline} (c.f. Peluso, 1995; Tsing, 2005).
[**These actions enabled** forestry, mining, military or agricultural
use to become established and allowed **capitalist modes of accumulation
by dispossession to occur**]{.underline} (Harvey, 2003). While volumes
and the subterranean emerge in contemporary geographic literature as
relevant to territorial expansion, the extension of nation-states into
new geological frontiers using geological mapping techniques is
sometimes discussed as if it were a practice of the past, restricted to
early-stage (colonial) politics and histories of territorialisation
(Braun, 2000; Scott, 2008; Himley, 2019; Marston, 2019). However,
[**geological** **mapping**]{.underline}, and indeed mapping of
frontiers, [is **not** something that's **finished.** Geographers have
certainly not exhausted the critical leverage that new perspectives in
territorial politics and materiality offer to understand the meanings
and implications of different cartographies.]{.underline}

Geological mapping is not finished because many places that were
inaccessible 30 or 50 years ago have been brought closer to global
networks of geological knowledge and practice via a combination of
technological advances, changes in land tenure regimes, and climate
change opening up previously ice-covered spaces. Examples of this
include deep sea geologies (Childs, 2020), Antarctic landmasses (Yusoff,
2005) and parts of the Canadian Arctic, as discussed in this thesis.
[Expansion of human knowledge into new geological frontiers is therefore
an ongoing process: geological territorialisation and enrolment into
expansionist imaginations is not over, nor are the practices that enable
this to happen.]{.underline}

[Since geological mapping in 'remote' areas of the Earth]{.underline}
(such as parts of the Canadian Arctic or the sea floor) [draws the
natural environment into closer contact with human interference, **areas
that previously avoided intense industrial activity now stand to be
impacted by it.** As such, there is **increased pressure to** evaluate
not only what 'work' geological knowledge is doing to **rationalise
expansion into new**]{.underline} (subterranean) [**territories**, but
also a need to understand what trade-offs are made during the great
pinching together of surface and subsurface space.]{.underline} After
all, [geological knowledge production and the desire-driven philosophy
of resource extraction occurs against a backdrop of other land use
values and philosophies]{.underline} (Simpson, 2019). Political ecology
is well equipped to untangle what happens when these things meet: where
communities, environment and resources come into contact. However, the
story does not end, nor necessarily begin, there. There is, for example,
a need to understand who is making the knowledge we have about geology
and resources, how this happens and to what effect. How is the practice
of geological knowledge production supporting contemporary regimes and
dreamscapes of state-making, and who benefits and gets left behind in
the process?

#### The alternative is a refusal of the 1AC. We refuse to recognize that which denies indigenous sovereignty as an act of ontological recalibration to expose the contingency and weakness of sovereign power.

**Thomas 24** (Deborah A. Thomas, R. Jean Brownlee Professor of
Anthropology in the Department of Anthropology at the University of
Pennsylvania; 7-15-24; "Refusal (and Repair)", Annual Review of
Anthropology Volume 53, 2024;
https://doi.org/10.1146/annurev-anthro-041422-020201) -- Langham EY

Within both critical Black and Indigenous studies, sovereignty has been
a vexed topic. Having defined itself as universal reason and absolute
perspectivity, [sovereignty is the interior humanity against which all
Others are compared and measured]{.underline} (and found wanting).
[Because **violence is the constitutive force of conquest, colonialism,
and slavery**]{.underline}---[and because within classic formulations of
political philosophy, **control over violence defines and legitimizes
the sovereign**]{.underline}---[**sovereignty is grounded within
historical-ideological** and onto-epistemological **phenomena that
benefit its architects and their progeny, producing whiteness**,
maleness, and Europeanness **as the apex of humanity**, the epitome of
transparency, universality, determination, and causality]{.underline}
(Ferreira da Silva 2007, Kauanui 2008, Sturm 2002, Wynter 2003).
[**Sovereignty operates by solidifying boundaries, controlling movement,
demanding a singularity of life** out of a vast multiplicity, and
creating and then **naturalizing hierarchies that function through
dehumanization, dispossession, and exclusion.** These **processes
advance through** the ongoing and simultaneous **recognition and
elimination of Indigenous populations**]{.underline} (Morgensen 2011,
Wolfe 2006), [through the **constitution of Black people as dispossessed
and** therefore **depoliticized subjects**]{.underline} (Spillers 1987),
[and through the standardization of dominant settler reckonings of
time]{.underline} (Bruyneel 2007, Rifkin 2017) [and relationship to
place]{.underline} (Harris 1993). How then can one enact
self-determination when new forms of dispossession are continuously
rewritten over earlier removals and displacements, what Shanya Cordis
(2019) would call the "settler ascription of sovereign in/capacity" (p.
20)?

It is important to remember that **[sovereignty is]{.underline}**, as
Joanne Barker (2005) has argued, "**[historically
contingent]{.underline}**. . .[**embedded within** the specific
**social** **relations** in which it is invoked and given
meaning]{.underline}" (p. 21). [Thus, it is **not unitary** but
instead]{.underline} "**[messy and incomplete]{.underline}**," as Circe
Sturm (2017, p. 343) has argued; relatedly, sovereignty has been
described as interdependent (Cattelino 2008, 2023), nested (Simpson
2014), and entangled (Dennison 2012). It is also important to remember
that [**sovereignty is obsessed with security because its conquest** and
disavowal of interiority **are never seamless** nor complete; it can be,
and often is]{.underline}, "[unhinged]{.underline}" (Thomas & Masco
2023), [**always potentially undone by that which fails to recognize
it**, by **that which refuses it** in intentional and unconscious ways.
Critical scholars within Black studies and Indigenous studies have
encouraged us to look at the question of sovereignty beyond the state
and beyond the parameters of its institutions, positioning it instead as
practices, performances, and processes that refuse law and dominion and
that draw attention to alternative ontological modes of governance,
community, and ceremony.]{.underline} In this way,
[they]{.underline}---like those in the previous section---[challenge
Western parameters of Being and becoming]{.underline} (see also Biehl &
Locke 2017).

Building from her classic articulation of refusal in Mohawk Interruptus,
[Audra Simpson]{.underline} (2014), for example, [has attempted to
distinguish sovereignty as a Western form of exceptionalism and
dominance from "**sovereignty as Indigenous belonging, dignity, and
justice**"]{.underline} (Simpson 2020, p. 686). In the latter context,
[the language of sovereignty has been central to Indigenous claims for
self-governance whether these claims are being presented to other
Indigenous nations, to other governments]{.underline} (such as the
United States or Canada), or to international tribunals. [Sovereignty
matters]{.underline}, therefore, [because it is a way to talk about an
ontology that makes land and water central to personhood, safety, and
integrity. In this **ontological recalibration**]{.underline}, Simpson
argues, [where easy spatial and temporal binaries and **Western
hierarchies** of being are **refused, sovereignty becomes a form of
relationality rather than an instrument of exclusion and violence**, a
logic for reproducing colonial rule through the perpetuation of the
tropes of cultural and temporal difference.]{.underline}

Similarly, Nishnaabeg scholar and writer Leanne Simpson (2017) discusses
[Indigenous governing structures as processual and emergent, a system of
leadership that reflected the local landscape. The idea of nationhood
that emerges from this relationality of context, land, and people
reflects what she calls an "ecology of intimacy"]{.underline} (p. 8),
[one grounded in connection, relationship, reciprocity, respect, and
self-determination]{.underline} (see also Maynard & Simpson 2022). For
Leanne Simpson, and for others, [**sovereignty cannot be rooted in
recognition** by the political formation **of the nation-state** because
it is inherent and unceded]{.underline} (Moreton-Robinson 2007).
Instead, [**sovereignty emerges from** the kind of]{.underline}
"**[grounded normativity]{.underline}**" [that]{.underline} Glen
Coulthard (2014) has argued [**grows through resurgent political** and
cultural **practices.** These are]{.underline} what Laura Harjo (2019)
identifies as "[practices of futurity]{.underline}" (p. 13)---[everyday
**community-based practices through which people recognize their own
power** to act and to self-determine]{.underline}---[practices that
**bring** a]{.underline} "**[radical sovereignty]{.underline}**" **[into
being.]{.underline}** Conscious efforts toward language revitalization
are among the ways to elaborate this kind of sovereignty, one that can
also redound to new forms of economic autonomy (Davis 2018; see also
Cattelino 2008). But so, too, are the kinds of rejections Larry Nesper
(2002) addresses in his account of Ojibwe spearfishers in Wisconsin who
"violated" fishing regulations in their observation of off-reservation
rights that had been guaranteed by nineteenth-century treaties with the
federal government. Other Native North American struggles for dignity,
land, water, and self-preservation---**[refusals of the state's refusal
of Native sovereignty]{.underline}**---[have similarly been
criminalized]{.underline} (Barker 2021), [yet tribes continue to
**challenge** juridical **norms that perpetuate human/nonhuman divides**
through the language of ownership]{.underline} (e.g., Carroll 2015,
Estes 2019, Powell 2018, Todd 2018).

## Theory of Power

### Thesis---Settler Necropolitics

#### Settler colonialism is an ongoing structure of dispossession and genocide. Settler states sustain their sovereignty through necropolitical governance that exposes racialized populations to "death worlds" while assimilating resistance through symbolic appeals to progress to obscure the intentional architecture of violence. 

**Peacock 25** (Ethan Peacock, Honors Student Majoring in Philosophy
Concentration Law & Political Science at Georgia Southern University;
5-13-25; "Invasion as a Structure: Carceral Capitalism, Necropolitics,
and the Persistence of Settler-Colonialism", *Honors College Theses*.
1054.; https://digitalcommons.georgiasouthern.edu/honors-theses/1054) --
Langham EY

I argue that [**settler-colonialism is not a closed historical event but
a living structure**: a foundational system that continues to shape the
political, legal, and socio-economic conditions of the
contemporary.]{.underline} In the United States, settler-colonialism is
often misrepresented as a "bad chapter" of the nation's history, an
embarrassing but resolved episode overcome by liberal reform. In Canada,
reconciliation efforts have become performative gestures, symbolic
acknowledgments of past harm that leave intact the systems responsible
for ongoing dispossession and oppression. These [narratives]{.underline}
function to [obscure the reality that **settler-colonial power remains
fully operational, embedded in** **the** very **institutions**,
infrastructures, and ideologies **that organize modern
life.**]{.underline} Glen Sean Coulthard's foundational structural
critique that ["**invasion is a structure and not an event**" compels us
to recognize that settler-colonialism is not merely a historical
phenomenon that has concluded; it is an enduring structural condition
that continues to influence the political, legal, and socio-economic
realities of contemporary nation-states.]{.underline}1 I assert that
[**settler-colonialism** persists]{.underline} not only through overt or
visible forms of state violence but also through [a pervasive network of
ideological, economic, and carceral systems that marginalize, exploit,
**dehumanize**, and dispossess **racialized peoples under the guise of
democratic progress, inclusion, and security**. This paradox is
especially striking because **these practices unfold within
self-identified liberal democracies**, where ideals of equality,
justice, and pluralism are professed **even as racialized dispossession
and colonial domination are embedded into their** legal and
**institutional** **architectures.**]{.underline}

**[Settler states]{.underline}** do not **[govern]{.underline}** by
fostering life but [**by managing death and disappearance.** Their power
is enacted not only through spectacular violence but through **routine
legal, economic**, and carceral **systems** that **regulate populations
and secure settler sovereignty.** Prisons, pipelines, borders, and
militarized policing work together to structure dispossession and
marginalization as ordinary. The **rhetoric of** democracy, inclusion,
and **security masks the continued reproduction of racialized** and
colonial **hierarchies.** Settler-colonialism adapts, modernizing its
forms while preserving its core logics.]{.underline} Coulthard's concept
of [the "**politics of recognition**"]{.underline} in Canada [reveals
how **settler states** reproduce the structures of settler-colonial
power by **absorbing resistance through symbolic inclusion while
preserving territorial sovereignty.**]{.underline}2 In Discourse on
Colonialism, Aimé Césaire's analysis of [colonialism as a foundational
infrastructure of capitalism illustrates how dispossession and
racialization remain necessary for maintaining settler
hegemony.]{.underline}3 Angela Davis and Michelle Alexander illustrate
how the prison-industrial complex (PIC) and mass incarceration function
as mechanisms for a carceral arm of racial capitalism designed to
dehumanize, alienate, commodify, and render disposable racialized and
marginalized peoples.4 Achille Mbembe provides the necropolitical
framework to elucidate how [contemporary **sovereignty** is increasingly
**exercised through the strategic exposure of specific populations to
premature death, structural abandonment, and systemic
neglect.**]{.underline} By situating symbolic bureaucratic appeasement,
anti-DEI rhetoric, and the prison-industrial complex (PIC) within the
broader context of settler-colonial capitalism, this research will
demonstrate how [contemporary **governance** is not just about simply
controlling people and power but about **structuring dispossession and
systematizing manufactured precarity.**]{.underline}

This project synthesizes these frameworks with a series of interrelated
case studies: the repression of land defenders at Standing Rock, the
development of militarized police infrastructure like Cop City, the
institutional decay of Mississippi's carceral system, and the Israeli
occupation of Palestine. Each represents a different node in the global
system of settler-colonial violence, technologically sophisticated,
transnationally coordinated, and rhetorically legitimized as progress or
security. While this project draws connections across contexts such as
the United States, Canada, and Israel, it acknowledges that these states
would not self-identify as [settler-colonial regimes]{.underline};
instead, they [justify their actions through the language of national
security, democratic governance, and legal sovereignty.]{.underline}
This interpretive framework is therefore intentionally critical,
foregrounding the structural logics that persist beneath official
narratives. Across these case studies, [settler-colonial states do not
govern by securing life; they govern by manufacturing its
disappearance.]{.underline} Through prisons, pipelines, surveillance
towers, and carceral practices, I contend that [**settler states like**
**the United States**]{.underline}, Canada, and Israel [**sustain
themselves through overlapping mechanisms of symbolic recognition,
capitalist accumulation, and necropolitical governance.** These systems
do not merely fail marginalized populations; they are structured to
oppress and erase them.]{.underline} The prison-industrial complex, the
occupation of Palestine, and the institutional collapse of Parchman
Prison all demonstrate how **[states produce]{.underline}** what Mbembe
calls ["**death worlds**," **zones in which the capacity to live is
degraded by design.**]{.underline}5 [These zones are not accidental;
they are the product of intentional policies, spatial control, and
infrastructural neglect.]{.underline} They are sophisticated expressions
of settler-colonial sovereignty.

### Thesis---Western Man

#### Western Man defines the telos of modernity's political orientation and its subsequent onto-epistemic registers. This overrepresentation results in a mythological capture of biocentric logics, producing the instrumentality of racialized bodies and the Earth 

**Mbembe 2023** (Achille Mbembe is a Cameroonian historian and political
theorist who is a research professor in history and politics at the Wits
Institute for Social and Economy Research at the University of the
Witwatersrand \"Death Itself\| The Politics of Human Extinction.\" Every
Living Thing: The Politics of Life in Common 2023//Jean Devon Rousseau)

I haven't the space to detail the many critiques of Arendt's central
theses in The Human Condition, particularly since her writing on
plurality is not the location of this chapter but its place of
departure. But one critique is directly related to my argument: the gaps
and frictions in her work concerning identity and difference.38 Earlier,
I suggested that plurality is not extensively theorized in The Human
Condition because it is presented as a matter of fact. Yet the "Man" at
the heart of Arendt's plurality emerges not from a womb but from a
story. As Sylvia Wynter argues in her essay "Unsettling the Coloniality
of Being/Power/ Truth/Freedom," extending a line of anticolonial
thinking with roots in Frantz Fanon and Aimé Césaire, "[Man" is the
outcome of an effort to present a dominating subset of humanity as human
being, the unmarked, universal center of Homo sapiens, the referent that
depends on the exclusion of human Others for its legibility and
existence]{.underline}.39

[Man, in other words, has been the rhetorical, epistemological, and
ontological accomplishment of white, Western, Enlightenment
elites]{.underline}. In Wynter's tracing of [Man's historical emergence
under the conditions of theology, colonialism, modernity, and racism,
she offers a powerful reminder that appeals to species commonality in
Man's name are difficult to unravel from the discourses of hierarchy and
violent forms of domination and exclusion that gave him
birth]{.underline}. It is a mistake to say, however, that Wynter is
opposed to speaking of humanity as such, or what she calls an
"ecumenical" humanity.40 Rather, [she is opposed to the
overrepresentation of the "West's biocentric Man" in our stories of
human origins and human futures, "the incorporation of all forms of
human being into a single homogenized descriptive statement that is
based on the figure of the West's liberal monohumanist
Man."]{.underline} [The problem with this figure]{.underline},
"[ostensibly natural-scientific]{.underline}," she explains, [is that it
presumes that the human is "like all purely biological species, a
natural organism,]{.underline} . . . and therefore exists, as such, in a
relationship of pure continuity with all other living beings (rather
than in one of both continuity and discontinuity)."41

## Links

### L---Adak

#### Adak island belong to the indigenous Aleut people -- The AFF is [literal]{.underline} land-grabbing. 

**Aleut Corp No date,** No date, "Aleut Lands: Adak"
https://aleutcorp.com/lands/adak/ LEHS.json

**[Adak Island is the traditional home to the Unangax̂ (Aleut)
people]{.underline}**, who lived for centuries within the rugged and
wild environment of the Aleutian Islands. Adak Island was populated by
Unangax̂ people until the 1830s when permanent villages were abandoned,
and the island was then used for subsistence. In 1942, during World War
II, the Adak Army Airfield was established for defensive action against
Japanese forces occupying Attu and Kiska. In 1945 the facilities were
transferred to Alaskan Air Command and then transferred again to the
U.S. Navy in 1950 until the facility was closed on March 31, 1997,
through the Base Realignment and Closure Program of 1995.

On March 17, 2004, [**Aleut received** 47,150 acres]{.underline} (about
twice the area of Manhattan) [of land and many repurposed **facilities
on Adak Island as the result of a Land Transfer Agreement with the
United States Government** facilitated by the Department of the Interior
under public Law Order 7609.]{.underline}

### L---Carbon Removal 

#### Carbon removal technology causes indigenous dispossession from their land, creating a racialized distribution of environmental damage. The 1AC's willingness to sacrifice black and indigenous bodies conditions genocide as the telos of climate adaptation. 

Dr. Holly Jean **Buck et al. 24**, Assistant Professor of Environment
and Sustainability at the University at Buffalo, "Racial Capitalism's
Role in Mitigation Deterrence from Carbon Removal," *Environmental
Science & Policy*, vol. 160,
https://doi.org/10.1016/j.envsci.2024.103865, DJDubb

How [environmental racism intersects with carbon removal]{.underline} is
crucial to understand and address. [However, the issue
isn't]{.underline} just [about disproportionately shared impacts or
benefits]{.underline} --- [which policy discussions]{.underline} in the
US are starting to [deal with]{.underline} more explicitly --- [but the
way that production of difference is central to the accumulation of
capital.]{.underline} These differences are perpetuated when carbon
removal technologies serve to extend prevailing fossil-based
accumulation regimes, a concern commonly known as mitigation deterrence.
In carbon removal policy discussions, it has become standard to mention
the danger of mitigation deterrence, which leads to suggestions of
policy remedies like separate targets. There are also discussions of
environmental justice, which lead to conversations on topics like
community engagement or cumulative burden assessment. Yet by the time
these concepts are addressed by companies and governments, environmental
racism is rarely directly mentioned. Scholarship can help make the link
here, and yet most articles on mitigation deterrence do not explicitly
address race either.

Here, some definition of terms will be useful for readers from multiple
disciplinary backgrounds:

• Mitigation deterrence is "the prospect of reduced or delayed
mitigation resulting from the introduction or consideration of another
climate intervention" (Markusson et al., 2018).

• Capitalism is a system in which private actors own the means of
producing goods, and extract profit from workers who are paid wages for
their labor. Following Nancy Fraser, we view capitalism not as a mere
economic system but as an institutionalized societal order, like
feudalism, which relies on structural divisions and institutional
separations (Fraser, 2022).

• Racism is not a collection of individual or intentional acts, but a
structural phenomenon, in which differentiation of people is core.
Racism is "the state-sanctioned or extra-legal production and
exploitation of group-differentiated vulnerability to premature death,"
as defined by Ruth Wilson Gilmore (Gilmore, 2007).

Racial capitalism, then, is a concept that examines how capitalism
relies on this production of difference. In this paper, we focus on
race, but the line of critique could be broadened to other differences
like gender or class. "Racial capitalism captures the sense that
actually existing capitalism exploits \[people\] through culturally and
socially constructed differences such as race, gender, region and
nationality and is lived through those uneven formations", writes Lisa
Lowe, quoted by Laura Pulido (Lowe, 2015; Pulido, 2016). Racial
capitalism is a concept whose origins lie with the intellectuals writing
about apartheid in the 1970s; it was popularized more recently in Cedric
Robinson's writing about the emergence of the black radical tradition,
and is now used in multiple fields --- with a core meaning that racism
and racial inequality and capitalism are deeply connected (Go, 2021).
Robinson saw capitalism emerging from the feudal order; it was racial
because racialism was already a part of European feudal society (Kelley,
2017, Taíw´ o\` and Bright, 2020). "Capitalism arose under a racist
European feudal system. It used "race" as a form of rule -- to
subordinate, to kill, and to enslave others -- and used that difference
for profit-making" (Estes, 2019, p. 28). Of course, this is not only a
historical system, but a present reality ---one that new technologies
and approaches like carbon removal evolve within. As one example of how
racial capitalism functions, Indigenous scholar and writer Nick Estes
points to how billionaire media tycoon Ted Turner owns more than 2
million acres of ranch land across the globe and more than 200,000 acres
of Oceti Sakowin treaty land in western South Dakota; i.e., a single
white man owns more wealth and land than entire Indigenous nations.

Racial capitalism is an important concept to understand for people
thinking about carbon removal. When we live in a society with not just
capitalism, but racial capitalism, [the default implementations of new
technologies and practices will be racialized]{.underline}. Here, we
follow the example of Laura Pulido's analysis of Flint, Michigan, in
which she illustrates that the catastrophe of the lead in Flint's water
supply cannot be explained by a nonracial material analysis, nor by
racism alone (Pulido, 2016). Racism made it possible to consider the
people in Flint disposable; the globalization of capital, the
deindustrialization of Flint, and austerity politics by the neoliberal
fiscal manager, appointed by the governor to replace elected officials
and return the city to fiscal solvency, also contributed to the
poisoning, as Pulido illustrates. Similarly, [damages from the
implementation of carbon removal under the status quo]{.underline} ---
[disenfranchised Black communities in the US suffering for UK climate
goals]{.underline}, in the case of Drax --- aren't explainable simply by
racism or capitalism alone. We have to understand the way in which
capitalism exploits difference in order to see what is going on and
address it.

It is becoming more common (at least in advocacy if not in scholarship)
to describe how [CDR infrastructures, impacts, and financial flows that
arise within a condition of structural racism]{.underline} are going to
[tend towards harmful projects --- this is important]{.underline}
analysis, [but]{.underline} it [only describes part of the problem. If
we see the problem that way, as the US state currently does, the
remedies]{.underline} might [look like]{.underline} something like
Justice40 ([a Biden]{.underline} Administration [Executive Order that
proscribes]{.underline} that 40 % of [the benefits from clean energy
investments flow to disadvantaged communities]{.underline}).
[Or]{.underline} it might look like New York State's recent legislation
[stipulating that projects]{.underline} with environmental impact [need
to do cumulative impacts analysis to receive environmental
permits]{.underline} --- an analysis of not just the impacts from the
proposed project, but an analysis that takes into account the existing
harms from other established activities. These are good steps, in that
they deal explicitly with recognizing disproportionate benefits, and
past and existing burdens. [But they don't fully get to the
ways]{.underline} in which [racial capitalism is producing these
differences, not just exploiting them. Nor do these steps
meet]{.underline} some of the [deeper concerns]{.underline} that
environmental and climate justice advocates have [around the overall
speed and trajectories of the energy transition, particularly around a
transition that allows fossil fuel companies and other polluters to
continue to profit.]{.underline} In other words, [analyses of
"responsible" or "harmful" CDR]{.underline} that focus only on
structural racism will [only take us partway towards understanding the
problems with carbon removal under business-as-usual sociopolitical
conditions.]{.underline}

In this short communication, we wish to make the specific point that
racial capitalism has direct implications for mitigation deterrence.
[Mitigation deterrence]{.underline} can [arise from CDR precisely
because the carbon sequestered by removal methods is being made to seem
equivalent to other carbon emitted]{.underline} (McLaren, 2016), [in
spite of many important differences in terms of origin]{.underline}
(fossil fuels, land use change etc.), [timing and geography]{.underline}
(Carton et al., 2021). Also, [the promise of carbon removal]{.underline}
can [lead to over-optimistic reliance on it]{.underline} (in good faith,
or not), and [carbon removal may fail to materialize or cause indirect
increase in emissions]{.underline}, all of [which would undermine
effective equivalence]{.underline}, and so substitution of CDR for
emissions reductions (McLaren, 2020).

Such equivalence is central to carbon budget science, and essential for
the notion of 'net zero' that is the lynchpin for how climate policy is
presented today. Equivalence is also [required for any offsetting of
hard to-abate emissions with CDR, whether through
commercial]{.underline} offsetting schemes [or]{.underline} the
[market-based policy instruments]{.underline} that are so strongly
favored in the current capitalist society.

However, it's not just that mitigation deterrence emerges because fossil
fuel capitalism has a financial interest in delaying mitigation --- it's
not only a product of capitalism, nor of conceptual accounting
practices, as described above. Race, gender, and class are a
constitutive part of how mitigation deterrence emerges.

In what follows, we clarify some of the ways in which not just racism or
capitalism but racial capitalism intersects with mitigation deterrence.
We distinguish between two main dynamics. In the first part of this
commentary, we look at how racial capitalism helps create the conditions
for mitigation deterrence. In the second, we look at how mitigation
deterrence in turn goes on to reproduce structural racism. This short
perspective piece is not intended to as "fill" the gap that we identify,
as deeper co-produced research would be needed to really understand how
race interacts with mitigation deterrence. Neither is this piece
"opening a conversation", given that environmental justice advocates
have been identifying concerns about CDR, environmental racism, and the
prospect of delayed mitigation for some years now. Nor is this the first
paper to discuss race in conjunction with CDR (for example, Batres et
al., 2021). However, the specific literature on mitigation deterrence
has not engaged much with race, and the way it has conceptualized
capitalism has not been explicit about how it produces and thrives on
difference between groups (see for example Markusson et al., 2018;
Markusson et al., 2022). For example, a Web of Science query found 34
papers on "mitigation deterrence", but none for "mitigation
deterrence" + race or racism; similarly, there are 36 papers found with
"moral hazard" + carbon removal, but none of those discuss race or
racism either. We argue that this is a problematic omission that
warrants more attention from scholars in this field.

We have been reflecting on this gap in the literature as guest editors
of this special issue on mitigation deterrence and carbon removal. We
wish to highlight our positionality: we are white scholars in the global
North, and do not have specific expertise on race. However, we wanted to
write this commentary because we saw this lack in the collection that we
were in charge of curating, and we also think it our responsibility to
reflect on why this gap exists and what it means for research on this
topic.

What we want to do here is both acknowledge the gap in the scholarly
literature, and to write down clearly and accessibly how race connects
to mitigation deterrence from carbon removal in a way that makes sense
--- not just to scholars, but also to policymakers and companies working
in this space. We have interacted with companies who hear statements
from civil society organizations like "CDR is environmental racism," and
they feel bewildered about how their engineered pilot project or
demonstration facility could be racist (as racism is still often
understood by many non-academics as a matter of individual intentional
acts, rather than as a structural force). We hope that social science
can play something of a translational role to help developers and
researchers consider the broader social context of their work. We want
to do this in a manner that is self-reflective about the act of research
and scholarship, and its contributions and limitations.

The paper draws on cases of racial environmental injustice from mainly
the US, where they are most extensively documented, whilst the
literature on mitigation deterrence stems from Northern Europe. This
geographical disparity may go some way towards explaining the disconnect
between the two literatures that we seek to address here.

2\. How mitigation deterrence is enabled by racial capitalism

There are at least two ways in which [mitigation deterrence is enabled
by racial capitalism]{.underline}. The first involves [global erasures
in modelling and discourse]{.underline} that [contribute to mitigation
deterrence. IPCC models]{.underline} that [substitute carbon removal for
emission reductions]{.underline} do so [because they prioritize the most
cost-effective mitigation options]{.underline} (Emmerling et al., 2019).
[Yet differences in the costs of mitigation]{.underline} around the
world are not accidental -- they at least partly [reflect structural
inequalities and unequal power relations between North and South, and
derive from the injustices of]{.underline} [historical and present
exploitation]{.underline}. By conflating such issues with the seemingly
neutral category of costs, the [models normalize and
ultimately]{.underline} help [reproduce these conditions]{.underline}
(Kanitkar et al., 2024). In an analysis of integrated assessment
modelling scenarios, Kantikar and colleagues for example found that
"across all scenarios, the current global inequalities in incomes,
energy use, and emissions, are projected to continue even in 2050.
Scenarios also project higher per capita fossil fuel use and lower
carbon dioxide removal in developed countries in 2050 compared to
developing countries" (Kanitkar et al., 2024). CDR is assumed to be
available in marginalized and Black communities because global
historical injustices that these groups were exposed to are projected to
continue.

[Erasures happen with both technological and "nature-based" carbon
removal, in]{.underline} different ways. With engineered removals,
[choosing]{.underline} sites in [locations like the US Gulf South for
first-of-a-kind projects]{.underline} risks failing to recognize the
communities who have [and]{.underline} are disproportionately
experiencing harms from fossil fuel and petrochemical production. This
lack of recognition may also happen when it comes to [engineered carbon
removals that would utilize Indigenous lands]{.underline}; for example,
in Canada, one analysis indicated that there is little evidence of
Indigenous engagement in direct air capture and storage, even though
storage activities occur in areas that are subject to Aboriginal and
Treaty rights (Cortinovis et al., 2024).

When it comes to nature-based removals, [studies that identify
"available" locations for]{.underline} land-based [carbon
sequestration]{.underline} (Griscom et al., 2017; Bastin et al., 2019;
Pozo et al., 2020) [find large potentials in]{.underline} poor nations
of [the global South and, in the process, erase the practices and
existence of pastoralists and Indigenous peoples]{.underline} who are
using that land (Bluwstein and Cavanagh, 2023). In other words, the way
of seeing that turns land and ecosystems into carbon is racialised.
Baldwin explains how deterritorialising the boreal forest as a space of
carbon operates as a "disciplinary schema" that consolidates an idea of
"white" national subjectivity (Baldwin, 2009). [This enacts a geography
of racial difference that constitutes epistemic violence, that is, the
erasure or marginalization of other ways of seeing and knowing the
world. The "invisibility" of racialized groups]{.underline} that live on
the land is part of what [enables]{.underline} the [land to be
considered]{.underline} as [a carbon removal resource, and]{.underline}
that imagined availability --- [calculations of]{.underline} the
potential of carbon removal --- can [result in projections of an
unrealistically large carbon removal potential]{.underline} (Dooley et
al., 2022). Over-reliance on [potential future carbon
removal]{.underline} is in turn likely to [deter current efforts to
reduce emissions]{.underline}.

There are ways to guard against these erasures. There are some examples
of researchers grappling with how to do technical assessment of carbon
removal that considers race and addresses erasures--- for example, the
recent analysis of carbon removal potential in the US by several
national laboratories, Roads to Removal, is spatially explicit to the
county level and includes mapping of Black-owned farms. As the report
states, "Historically the US agricultural industry was built on
injustice and inequity, including relying on enslaved farmworkers; white
theft of land owned by indigenous, Black, and other people of color; and
bank and crop-insurance discrimination against Black, indigenous, and
farmers of color... These historic and ongoing injustices have resulted
in an agricultural industry composed of more than 95 % white (and
majority male) land owners and 86 % white land renters" (Pett-Ridge et
al., 2023). The report uses county-level data to map the acres of
agricultural land operated with non-white farmers as a recommendation to
target equity-based outreach to farmers, and the report also maps
non-white and Tribal woodland areas, grounding the problem of forest
carbon loss in context, explaining that historic forest loss happened
due to settler colonial expansion that "violently separated Indigenous
communities from the forests they stewarded." Along with discussions of
Indigenous cultural fire practices and current Indigenous forest
stewardship, this sets up the report to identify both potential
synergies and conflicts between Indigenous forest management and
forest-based carbon removal. This not to say the report is perfect, but
it was not always the case that technical government-funded reports
could talk about theft of land --- there is an opportunity here for
social scientists to collaborate with modelers and continue the
conversation about how to do this work. Of course, there is a difference
between emerging best practices in the public sector, and what global
companies will likely default to as the industry grows. Until better
modelling practices are fully institutionalized around the world and
also used by private actors, the (racialized) mitigation deterrence risk
remains.

A second way that [racial capitalism enables mitigation
deterrence]{.underline} is [through racialized labor formations. Common
assumptions]{.underline} of the cost of [carbon removal also contain
assumptions about labour]{.underline} costs, [and these]{.underline}
assumptions [have racial implications.]{.underline} For example,
[inexpensive carbon removal]{.underline} may [hinge on]{.underline}
being able to do it in [places where wages are cheaper]{.underline}.
Take [for example]{.underline} the seaweed industry: it is difficult to
scale up a seaweed industry in the global North due to labor costs
compared with the established seaweed industry in [Indonesia and
China]{.underline}. Seaweed sinking as a carbon removal approach, should
it be effective, may face similar economic dynamics. Likewise, tree
planting might appear economical in places such as [sub-Saharan
Africa]{.underline} because of low-paid labour.

There is a whole line of scholarship on the "Plantationocene",
describing alienation of land and labor and the long-distance
simplification of landscapes, with the plantation as a race-based social
system embodying both racial violence and resistance. This scholarship
argues [the Plantationocene]{.underline} is a more accurate term than
the Anthropocene to describe an era that was [characterized by
Indigenous dispossession of lands, settler colonialism, slavery, and
racialized distribution of environmental change]{.underline} (Wolford,
2021). Although not without its own issues,1 this lens [helps us
see]{.underline} which [racialized labor formations will likely emerge
in a CDR industry that relies on]{.underline} large-scale, standardized
land management to remove carbon. Even though soil carbon, enhanced
weathering, afforestation, or biomass companies may be possible through
visions of small or community-scale carbon removal, the [market
imperatives to deliver carbon removal cheaply]{.underline} [and with
standardized measurement]{.underline} complicate alternative
formulations of it.

If fair wages were priced into labour-intensive forms of removal, then
maybe mitigation would be cheaper than CDR at least in certain sectors.
This is understudied but should be a subject of research. To enable such
studies, it is crucial that models become more transparent about the
kinds of assumptions, e.g. costs of labour and land, that go into the
modelling of CDR.

Finally, [mitigation deterrence might arise through the lack of
representation in defining, articulating, and projecting
CDR]{.underline}. Certainly, one might go to a CDR event and see women
and people of color, and all-male panels are becoming rare. There are
some women and people of color founders, and many people working on
policy, research, and finance. Yet in respect to where funding comes
from (and returns to) --- it appears that [climate tech]{.underline} so
far [remains disproportionately white, male, and global North
dominated.]{.underline} This leads to a situation where women take
enabling roles while male executives and investors are set up to profit
from the industry's development. The question of representation also
arises when [CDR policy favors incumbent industries, players, or methods
tied to legacy interests]{.underline}, rather than some of these nascent
startups. [Social difference ends up being reproduced in]{.underline}
many of [these scenarios]{.underline}. Again, we are not aware of
research exploring these issues at the moment, yet it is clear that they
raise important questions for mitigation deterrence scholarship. Could
[a lack of representation lead to overconfidence in the possibilities of
carbon removal, and a blindness to negative impacts]{.underline}? Does
[the dominance of (white, male, global North dominated) science,
technology and engineering]{.underline} and the underfunding of social
dimensions or sustainability systems research [lead to framings like
"the need for 10 Gt", and the blindness to]{.underline} some of the
[on-the-ground challenges]{.underline} of implementing new
land-intensive practices, in ways that deter emissions reductions?

3\. How mitigation deterrence in turn also perpetuates racial capitalism

Racial capitalism enables mitigation deterrence, but it is also the case
that mitigation deterrence feeds back and helps perpetuate racial
capitalism. The idea of [compensating]{.underline} for [ongoing
emissions with removals on the open market furthers environmental
racism, by enabling unspecified ongoing emissions to be compensated by
whomever is able to pay]{.underline} --- especially in the absence of a
framework specifying how to allocate residual emissions.

Environmental justice scholars and activists have for decades
demonstrated the racialized politics behind the siting of industrial
plants (Taylor, 2014; Greenpeace, 2022; Roy, 2020). It is well
documented that the burdens of pollution fall disproportionately on
communities of color within countries like the US, and also globally
(see e.g. (Liu et al., 2021). But scholars of race and environmental
justice such as John D. Marquez ´ have also pointed out the ways in
which this society constructs people of color as expendable (Marquez, ´
2014) --- not just in localized "sacrifice zones", but in entire
populations (Pellow, 2017).

[It is because of]{.underline} the [systemic devaluation]{.underline} of
BIPOC lives --- and the correspondent siting of industrial facilities
--- [that society is able to entertain]{.underline} the idea of
[compensating for industrial fossil fuel emissions]{.underline}, or
luxury emissions like those of business class-air travel that
disproportionately are created by and benefit rich, upper class White
men --- [with carbon removals. Such practices make it possible for
polluting refineries]{.underline} that produce jet fuel, likely to be
[sited in a community of color, to keep on running]{.underline}. It is
one of the reasons why communities of color already overburdened by
pollution from the petrochemical industry in places such as Southwestern
Louisiana and Texas are concerned that siting [direct air capture hubs
there will worsen environmental risks and burdens]{.underline}. Among
their concerns are the [risks that derive from new CO2 transport
infrastructure, as well as the possibility of extending the life
of]{.underline} polluting [fossil fuelled industrial
facilities]{.underline} (Scott-Buechler et al., 2023). While in theory
the risks from new infrastructure are manageable with proper regulation,
these communities have a history of experiencing improper regulation and
lax enforcement at state and federal levels. If direct air capture
projects either add to the cumulative burden or keep polluting
facilities operational, then [BIPOC communities would again be
sacrificed]{.underline} --- [this time to]{.underline} national,
foreign, or corporate [decarbonization goals.]{.underline}

Of course, most carbon removal project proponents and companies would
say that is not the idea behind their work. They would also say that
they intend to build good projects with the best available control
technologies for any non-CO2 emissions. Yet [if carbon removal policy
allows for the continued operation of industrial facilities]{.underline}
that are disproportionately sited [in communities of color]{.underline}
--- [and]{.underline} if [companies are allowed]{.underline} [to
counterbalance]{.underline} their [emissions with removals]{.underline},
rather than decarbonizing their processes while upgrading to the latest
pollution control technologies --- [then it is complicit in
racism]{.underline}. The lack of policy to avoid such situations is
tantamount to silently endorsing them. (Notably, mitigation policy that
leads to the closure of industrial facilities in rich countries while
offshoring that pollution to other countries can also be an expression
of racism.)

[Black, Indigenous, people]{.underline} of colour, women, and low-income
people are underrepresented in the institutions making decisions about
carbon removal and profiting from those decisions, and they are doubly
and [disproportionately impacted by mitigation deterrence]{.underline}
dynamics. Doubly because these groups bear the brunt of the negative
impacts of both CDR initiatives [and]{.underline} the activities that
remain unabated owing to mitigation deterrence dynamics. If we also add
[climate change impacts]{.underline}, then they [might be triply
impacted, especially if carbon removal]{.underline} is first relied on
but then [fails to live up to its climate-altering promise.]{.underline}

### L---Care

#### The 1AC utilizes care to govern indigenous life through false promises of hope while simultaneously rendering them disposable and thus open to death.

**Reid 22** (Julia Reid, professor at the University of Lapland,
Rovaniemi, Finland; 10/2/22 "Biopolitics" published in "Critical Studies
of the Arctic"; DOI: https://doi.org/10.1007/978-3-031-11120-4_12) --
Langham EY

Biopolitics of the Arctic

The study of biopolitics in the Arctic is relatively under-developed
when we compare it with the study of geopolitics. We know much by now
about the Arctic as a space of geopolitical contestation between
powerful territorially aggrandizing states (Tamnes & Offerdal, 2014). We
know much less about [the **Arctic** as a space of]{.underline}
specifically **[biopolitical governance]{.underline}**, a space, that
is, [in which **power** is **exercised** over human populations
**through** strategies of **care** for life.]{.underline} The need for
such understanding and analysis is all the greater in this present era
of pandemics and the threat of disease, which is to say, of biological
death, of suffering and of loss and illness, in the Arctic as much as
elsewhere.

However, there are examples of research in which the concept of
biopolitics has been deployed to make sense of how power is organized
and exercised in the Arctic. The anthropologist Lisa Stevenson has
written especially important works in which she has developed the
concept as a framework for understanding power relations between the
Canadian state and the Inuit who live in Arctic Canada (Stevenson,
2012). Rather than try to provide a broad survey of different texts in
which the concept of biopolitics has been deployed to approach power in
the Arctic, this chapter will focus specifically on Stevenson's work, as
an important and thorough example of scholarship on Arctic biopolitics.
It is a text which does not simply add or transfer biopolitics to Arctic
Studies from other areas of research, but which, in examining the
Arctic, contributes significantly to the fundamental theory and
understanding of the importance of biopolitics for knowledge. Yet it
also has limits, owing to its relative age and to its disciplinary
origins in anthropology; these require us to think beyond it, using
recent insights into the changing nature of biopolitics, drawn from
International Relations and elsewhere.

As Stevenson argues, one of the major utilities of the concept of
biopolitics for the study of power in the Arctic is the way in which it
enables us to reveal and see the violence of discourses and practices of
care in the Arctic. [The **biopolitics of care**]{.underline} is by now
a very wide subject area transcending disciplines and geographic areas
of study (De Boever, 2013). It is for obvious reasons a major feature of
critical humanitarian studies, as theorists and practitioners have
learnt to be more circumspect about the relations between humanitarian
care and power (Mavelli, 2017). We know well by now that the concept and
practice of care is deeply loaded, politically. Stevenson's study of the
Inuit experience of care in the Canadian Arctic is yet another
contribution to the wider understanding of how [**care**
functions]{.underline}, [in the Arctic]{.underline}, as well as many
other spaces of liberal intervention, [to **legitimate
intervention**]{.underline} itself (Reid, 2005).

One of the main ways in which [**Inuit** have **suffered from** the
**care** of the Canadian state]{.underline} has been [through the
intervention in the suicide crisis said to exist in Inuit communities.
The suicide crisis]{.underline} among the Inuit, [as well as the wider
**health problems** identified with Inuit communities, has been useful
to the Canadian state when it comes to **legitimating interventions** in
Inuit communities]{.underline}. It is not that the biopolitical state
does not care; it does. The point is, as Stevenson well argues, that [it
is useful for the state to care where **care legitimates** its
capacities to intervene and ensure its own **colonial control** of
spaces and peoples]{.underline}, including the Inuit.

Suicide crises in the Arctic are not limited to Inuit communities, and
the analysis of the biopolitical functions of these crises in
legitimating colonial interventions in populations and territories could
easily be extended to encompass other peoples and places. The 'increased
risk' of suicide among Saami populations has, for example, been
extensively researched, with particular attention drawn to the high
rates of suicide among young Saami men (Silviken et al., 2006). These
elevated rates are reported as being especially high among
reindeer-herding men, and higher still among young men (Sjolander,
2011). Attempts are ongoing to increase awareness and refine the means
by which the suicide crisis in Saami communities is addressed. A recent
study identified 17 different suicide prevention initiatives targeting
Saami in Norway, Sweden and Finland, as well as 40 different
problematizations regarding the question of how to prevent suicide among
Saami (Stoor et al., 2021). Typically, such **[studies]{.underline}**
are statistical and **[make]{.underline}** their own **[biopolitical
assumption]{.underline}** that suicide is a 'problem' and an [expression
of]{.underline} '[unfavourable]{.underline} psychosocial
[outcomes]{.underline}' [which require preventing]{.underline}
(Kiaerbech et al., 2021). In other areas of the Arctic, as diverse as
Alaska and the northern regions of Russia, and in the analysis of other
indigenous groups, the same [positivist approaches prevail, under the
guise of]{.underline} '[prevention science]{.underline}' (Allen et al.,
2022) [and]{.underline} '[targeted prevention]{.underline}' (Sumarokov
et al., 2014). **[Increasing]{.underline}** the efficacy of
**[interventions]{.underline}** is seen to [go hand in hand
**with**]{.underline} the need for [**increasingly refined**
quantitative **studies** of the whys and wherefores of
indigenous]{.underline} '[mental health]{.underline}' (Middleton et al.,
2021). Of all the different suicide crises researched in the Arctic it
is that of the Inuit that has drawn most attention for the high rates of
suicide, ones regularly detailed as being among the highest in the world
(Kral, 2016). Mean agestandardized analyses of different suicide rates
in the eight different Arctic states indicate that the three Arctic
regions with the very highest rates are Chukotka, Nunavut and Greenland,
all of which contain large indigenous populations (Kue-Young et al.,
2015). Statistical analyses, however, remain limited in their different
conclusions, partly because not all Arctic states record ethnic identity
in their health databases (Kue-Young et al., 2015). Nevertheless, [the
rising interest in documenting and detailing the rates and risks of
suicide in Arctic regions can be seen to have developed in parallel with
the scaling up of mental health care as a]{.underline} '[core
service]{.underline}' [of state **health agencies** as well as
non-governmental agencies **governing indigeneity in the Arctic** as a
whole]{.underline} (Trout & Wexler, 2020).

Thinking about the biopolitics of care is also fundamental to the work
of two other scholars of the Arctic---Marjo Lindroth and Heidi
SinevaaraNiskanen---whose work addresses it as a central theme (Lindroth
& Sinevaara-Niskanen, 2018, 2022). The focus of their research has been
on the continuities of colonialism at work in the policies of Arctic
states such as Canada, as well as the Arctic Council. This is important
because the Arctic Council is often identified as a more progressive and
postcolonial regime of governance when compared with the Arctic states.
As Lindroth and Sinevaara-Niskanen describe, the significance of the
Arctic Council is that it functions to represent a structural
transformation in the relations of states to indigenous peoples, on
account of its being more inclusive and consultative of indigenous
peoples than the states of the Arctic region (Lindroth &
Sinevaara-Niskanen, 2022: 16). Whether we are considering the care of
the Arctic Council or of Arctic states for indigenous peoples, [it is
essential]{.underline}, they argue, [that we see how such **care
functions strategically**]{.underline}, as a way of signalling to
indigenous peoples and the wider world that things are getting better.
[Care]{.underline}, they suggest, '[**signals hope** for the
better]{.underline}' [yet, on closer inspection, is **revealed to
be**]{.underline} '**[dangerous]{.underline}**' [and]{.underline} indeed
'**[colonial]{.underline}**' (Lindroth & Sinevaara-Niskanen, 2022: 101).
**[Hope]{.underline}** itself has to be understood [as another element
of]{.underline} such **[colonialism]{.underline}**, they argue, for
[acts of care are designed to]{.underline} '[reproduce
hope]{.underline}' (Lindroth & SinevaaraNiskanen, 2022: 101).
[Expressions of a will to **care** **enable** Arctic regimes to
implement a]{.underline} '**[politics of hope]{.underline}**'
[which]{.underline}, in turn, [**extends** their **colonial rule** over
indigenous peoples]{.underline} (Lindroth & Sinevaara-Niskanen, 2022:
101). As Claes Tängh Wrangel has well argued, [**hope**, like care, is a
**biopolitical technology**]{.underline} (Tängh Wrangel, 2019: 669);
[its function is to **enable regimes** of power **to govern** the
**life** of the populations which they target, and
**produce**]{.underline} '**[hopeful subjects]{.underline}**' [whose
**orientation** to the future is **secure** and **not** **dangerous to
political power**. **Subjects without hope**, it is **feared** in the
United States]{.underline}, for example, [are more likely to turn into
suicide bombers]{.underline} (Tängh Wrangel, 2019: 672). [In the Arctic,
there is a comparable set of linkages between care, hope and suicide
that are equally biopolitical]{.underline}. The Arctic Council has been
particularly proactive in tackling the suicide crisis in the Arctic, not
just among the Inuit but among wider indigenous communities as well. It
is essential therefore that the Council's policies towards suicide be
subjected to the same kind of biopolitical scrutiny that Stevenson has
given to those of the Canadian state.

Refusing Care

Understanding the biopolitical rationalities which shape the discourses
and practices of care in the Arctic also enables us to better understand
the rationalities of those who resist that care in the Arctic. It is a
fact that [while care continues to be exercised towards **indigenous
peoples** of the Arctic]{.underline}, [by Arctic states]{.underline} as
much as by the Arctic Council, [and while some representatives of those
peoples cooperate with these colonial bodies, others among these peoples
resist and **refuse** that **care**. The reason for that resistance and
**refusal** owes **to** the ways in which **care** is sometimes
experienced **as** **murderous**]{.underline} (Stevenson, 2014: 4).
Stevenson's [focus on the **disjuncture between** the **intention**
of]{.underline} the [care]{.underline} of Canada **[and]{.underline}**
the [Inuit **experience** of that care as murderous]{.underline} leads
her to ask what it is that goes on in this disjuncture between the
intention to care for life and the experience of that care as murderous.
Is there something in Inuit subjectivity that produces the anger of the
rejection of that care, a rejection which has, as she also well details,
produced a suicide epidemic in Nunavut in which the suicide rate for the
region is more than five times the Canadian national average (Stevenson,
2014: 7). What is it in the Inuit, as a subjectformation, as well as a
culture and people, and as a political subject, that produces this
apparent desire for death? Is there something in biopolitics and the
experience of the caring embrace of biopower that leads the Inuit to
reject it and choose death? These are questions which are not specific
to the study of the Arctic, or of indigenous peoples elsewhere. However,
they are key to the ways in which the concept of biopolitics has been
mobilized within Arctic research to date.

Answering these questions requires us to navigate the colonial origins
and development of biopower. Among the Inuit, [the experience of
biopolitics has been translated through the practices by which Canada
responded to the tuberculosis crisis]{.underline} of the 1950s and
1960s: [many **Inuit** were **forcibly** **removed** from their homes
and communities in the Arctic and shipped to sanitoria in the south of
the country, where they were **treated**, often **only to die without**
ever **seeing** their **homes** and families **again**.]{.underline} The
[inhumanity of]{.underline} those **[biopolitical
practices]{.underline}**, not just the force with which Inuit were
removed, but the ways in which their **[lives]{.underline}** were
**[reduced to statistical series]{.underline}**---[numbers and not
names]{.underline}---and to [diseased bodies to be treated and not
psyches with dreams and desires of their own]{.underline}, may help us
to understand their resistance and refusal. These biopolitical
techniques were effectively drawn, as Stevenson argues, from the realms
of animal husbandry (Stevenson 2014: 29). [The death of a tubercular
Inuit was often anonymous, leaving no trace in]{.underline} Canadian
state [records other than a simple index card, marked with the statement
'dead' and with the year of death enumerated]{.underline}---'1956' in
the case of one Inuit, Kaujak, whose journey to death Stevenson's
research attempts to unearth more details about (Stevenson, 2014:
21--46).

Superficial critics of Foucault's theory of biopolitics have often
pointed out that it is marked by an absence of an analysis of
colonialism. The argument goes that Foucault was a Eurocentric thinker
with little regard for the non-European world, and that his theory is,
in fact, as Howell and Richter-Montpetit argue, racist (Howell &
Richter-Montpetit, 2019: 2). Research on biopolitics, which uses the
concept to help us understand how care functions in the strategies of
colonial powers, is, therefore, truly important in overcoming this
analytical deficit of Foucault's. In the Arctic, at least, the
biopolitical emphasis of state power on caring for and preserving life
can be seen to be a fundamentally 'colonial norm' (Stevenson, 2014: 96).

Looking at practices of care through a biopolitical lens in these ways
is really to pose a challenge to the ever-larger literatures in the
health sciences that continue to treat care as if it were sheer
benevolence. [Identifying suicide simply as a problem to be solved and
enacting]{.underline} '[suicide prevention interventions]{.underline}'
[as the solution]{.underline}, as has been done by the Arctic Council
through its Rising Sun initiative since 2015 (Collins et al., 2019:
152), [may only reproduce the kinds of problems which earlier
interventions in indigenous communities are known for, namely the
experience of colonialism. Scientific studies]{.underline} which [argue
for **state-led** **interventions** **into communities** **where** the
act of **suicide is itself an indigenous solution to** the problem of
**colonial intervention** **do not**]{.underline} seem to [**see** the
**irony** and paradoxicality of their reasoning]{.underline} (Pollock et
al., 2020). [A **biopolitical analytic**]{.underline} can help in at
least **[making]{.underline}** such **[paradoxes]{.underline}** and
ironies [more **apparent**.]{.underline}

### L---Cartography

#### The 1AC's mapping of Arctic land is part and parcel with the ongoing colonial project of extractivism, whereby the production of geological knowledge serves to rationalize intervention and the dispossession of indigenous peoples from their land.

**Becker 22** (Matilda Becker, Strategic Partnerships Manager and a
Research Fellow at Oxford Net Zero; 2022; "Arctic mineral futures:
tracing geoscience knowledge from field to community in the Northwest
Territories, Canada";
https://ora.ox.ac.uk/objects/uuid:5220d418-281b-48d7-91de-8789fc8a149f)
-- Langham EY

[This thesis frames the subterranean as a space that is still in the
making and whose constitution is not yet settled. It views the
activities and products of **geological mapping as generative processes
that enable us to imagine the subterranean**, expand down into it and
draw it up into our surface worlds.]{.underline} As I will show in this
thesis, [**geological maps of the Arctic** today, as nearly 500 years
ago, contain reflections of societal meanings and aspirations of what
could or should become of a place, and resultingly, become a mechanism
to invent and **produce**]{.underline} (**[economic]{.underline}**)
**[spaces.]{.underline}**

[The production and realisation of imagined spaces is crucial to the
curation of socio-political and socio-environmental relations with
place.]{.underline} As political ecologists have noted, [**mapping** has
worked to **rationalise colonial interventions into colonised lands**
and supported the **transformation of lands'** existing ecosystems or
communities **into supporting** more **European, 'rationalised' forms of
spatial production**]{.underline} (c.f. Peluso, 1995; Tsing, 2005).
[**These actions enabled** forestry, mining, military or agricultural
use to become established and allowed **capitalist modes of accumulation
by dispossession to occur**]{.underline} (Harvey, 2003). While volumes
and the subterranean emerge in contemporary geographic literature as
relevant to territorial expansion, the extension of nation-states into
new geological frontiers using geological mapping techniques is
sometimes discussed as if it were a practice of the past, restricted to
