early-stage (colonial) politics and histories of territorialisation
(Braun, 2000; Scott, 2008; Himley, 2019; Marston, 2019). However,
[**geological** **mapping**]{.underline}, and indeed mapping of
frontiers, [is **not** something that's **finished.** Geographers have
certainly not exhausted the critical leverage that new perspectives in
territorial politics and materiality offer to understand the meanings
and implications of different cartographies.]{.underline}

Geological mapping is not finished because many places that were
inaccessible 30 or 50 years ago have been brought closer to global
networks of geological knowledge and practice via a combination of
technological advances, changes in land tenure regimes, and climate
change opening up previously ice-covered spaces. Examples of this
include deep sea geologies (Childs, 2020), Antarctic landmasses (Yusoff,
2005) and parts of the Canadian Arctic, as discussed in this thesis.
[Expansion of human knowledge into new geological frontiers is therefore
an ongoing process: geological territorialisation and enrolment into
expansionist imaginations is not over, nor are the practices that enable
this to happen.]{.underline}

[Since geological mapping in 'remote' areas of the Earth]{.underline}
(such as parts of the Canadian Arctic or the sea floor) [draws the
natural environment into closer contact with human interference, **areas
that previously avoided intense industrial activity now stand to be
impacted by it.** As such, there is **increased pressure to** evaluate
not only what 'work' geological knowledge is doing to **rationalise
expansion into new**]{.underline} (subterranean) [**territories**, but
also a need to understand what trade-offs are made during the great
pinching together of surface and subsurface space.]{.underline} After
all, [geological knowledge production and the desire-driven philosophy
of resource extraction occurs against a backdrop of other land use
values and philosophies]{.underline} (Simpson, 2019). Political ecology
is well equipped to untangle what happens when these things meet: where
communities, environment and resources come into contact. However, the
story does not end, nor necessarily begin, there. There is, for example,
a need to understand who is making the knowledge we have about geology
and resources, how this happens and to what effect. How is the practice
of geological knowledge production supporting contemporary regimes and
dreamscapes of state-making, and who benefits and gets left behind in
the process?

### L---Disease

#### Pandemics don't just spread illness, they support the state's power to determine who is killable. Colonized and racialized bodies are cast as biologically risky and socially disposable, enabling their abandonment, quarantine, or use as experimental subjects. Disease control enforces biopolitical order by managing who lives, who suffers, and who can be sacrificed in the name of public health. The logic of infection becomes a logic of elimination.

**Jiwani 24**, \[Dr. Yasmin Jiwani. Dr. Jiwani has a BA in Psychology
from the University of British Columbia, a MA in Sociology from Simon
Fraser University and a PhD in Communications from Simon Fraser. Jiwani
is a feminist academic and activist. In her research, she examines the
intersectionality of race and gender in media narratives of violence
against women and representations of racialized peoples. 3-28-2024.
Chapter 8. "Contagious Bodies: A Pandemic of Racism" Planetary Health
Humanities and Pandemics. (1st ed.). Routledge.
https://doi.org/10.4324/9781003367581\]//ins VZ ☼

Colonialism and COVID-19

In a roundtable discussion called "Epidemics in the Past and Now: A
Roundtable on Colonial and Postcolonial History," David Arnold remarks,
"The colonial past seems profoundly present in the current pandemic: it
even has the same resonances of class and coercive govern- ment, of
scapegoating and victimization. The colonial has both relevance and
resonance" (C. Anderson et al. 2021). The COVID- 19 pandemic is the
latest in a long line of biological infections to impact societies on a
global scale. Yet, despite its biological basis, COVID-19 is also
aligned with and sutured to social hierarchies that identify some bodies
as riskier than others--- risky bodies as contagious bodies that need to
be contained, neutralized, or expelled from the social and political
body of society. Indeed, as Alexandre White contends, "[the history of
international **infectious** **disease** **control** has largely been
shaped by a **distinctly** **European** **perspective**, prioritising
epidemic threats that arose from **colonial** (or now post- colonial)
**sites** **that** **threatened** **to** **spread** **disease** **and**
**affect** **trade**]{.underline}" (2020, 1251). Alongside other
scholars (e.g., Davis et al. 2019; Yusoff 2021), I would argue that
[**massive** and **dramatic** **planetary** **changes** have been
**accelerated** **by** **colonialism** and have resulted in the
**emergence** **and** **spread** **of** **different** **pandemics**.
Indeed, it was through the **colonial** **project** that
**enlightenment** **notions** **of** **the** **human** were used as the
standard to define the "**inhumanity** **of** **the** **colonial**
**subject**"]{.underline} (Yusoff 2021, 664). [**Those** considered
**nonhuman** **or** **subhuman** became, and continue to be, regarded as
**contagious** **bodies**--- risky bodies that are a source of contagion
but also fodder for **experimentation** **in** **the** **interests**
**of** **advancing** **Western** **medicine**]{.underline}.2

Indeed, as Charles Briggs argues, "[**narratives** about **epidemics**
make **racial** **and** **sexual** **inequalities** seem **natural**
**as** **if** **bacteria** **and** **viruses** gravitate toward
**populations** and respect **social** **boundaries**]{.underline}"
(2005, 272). [In the colonial context, **colonized** **subject**
**races** were essentialized in a similar fashion--- as having an
**essential** **proclivity** **to** **certain** **diseases** **and**
**an** **exceptional** **resistance** **to** **others**]{.underline} (W.
Anderson 1996). A well known case of the intersection of class, race,
gender, and immigration in the narrative construction of diseased
Others, who threaten the social body, is Mary Mallon, a poor Irish
immigrant woman in New York in the early 1900s who was patient zero in
the outbreak of typhoid. Mallon, who became known as Typhoid Mary, was
portrayed as a "mass-spreader" in the media and held in quarantine on
"Northern Brother Island for 26 years till her death" (Banerjee,
Kallivayalil, and Rao 2020, S103). A working class woman and Irish
immigrant (who were racialized at the time), Mallon was thus situated at
various axes of power that worked in concert to orchestrate her
victimization and ability to infect others in the different households
in which she worked. The link between colonialism and disease is
sustained from the attribution of the Bubonic plague to Jews by the
Catholic church in the thirteenth century to today's "epidemic
orientalism" (White 2023), symbolized by the naming of diseases such as
the Hong Kong flu; the Ebola virus, named after a river in the Congo;
the Zika virus, named after a forest in Uganda; and Middle Eastern
respiratory syndrome (see also Mallapragada 2021). More recently,
monkeypox has been used to target LGBTQ+ individuals, suggesting that
they are the primary carriers in spite of the reality that the disease
is common across genders and sexualities in Europe and Africa.3
Underpinning this **naming** **of** **diseases** is a **pattern** **of**
**structured** **dominance**, namely, [a whole architecture of how
**colonized** **bodies** have been **viewed**---not only as originators
and carriers of contgion but as **bestial**, **subhuman**, and
**nonhuman**. In fact, the **racialized** and **colonized** **humans**
**were** **seen** **as** **closer** **to** **animals**--- that is,
**nonhumans**--- and therefore more likely to **harbor** **zoonotic**
**diseases**.]{.underline}

The twinning discourses of immigration with germs and diseases, which
has been so prevalent in the case of severe acute respiratory syndrome
(SARS) and, more recently, COVID-19, is indicative of how geography,
race, and sexuality are entangled in discourses about diseases (Dionne
and Turkmen 2020). The planetary reach of these definitional categories
and originary frameworks linking disease to colonized bodies and
territories is apparent in the responses of global health organizations
such as the World Health Organization (WHO) to what Nicholas King
defines as the "emerging diseases worldview" (King 2002). To some
extent, he argues, these organizations were created as part of the
civilizing mission with the mandate of surveilling and tracking disease
transmission from colonized territories and implementing controls that
would impede movement from the affected regions of the world to
metropolitan centers of power such as the United States.4 In this
regard, "the colonial project was not only an attempt to establish a
political network, but also to export a vision of bounded geographic
space worldwide" (King 2002, 772). In fact, as Luka Blair points out,
"disease animated much of the colonial discourse on placehood, formed a
central basis for colonial infrastructure, and enabled a moral-
political justification for colonial interventions" (C. Anderson et al.
2021). [Thus, the **securitization** **and** **governance** **of**
**disease** **was** **instrumental** not only to the **perpetuation**
**of** **colonial** **relationships** **of** **power** but also to the
**militarization** **of** **the** **Global** **North** in the wake of
the 9/11 terrorist attacks. Indeed, an inherent part of the **emerging**
**disease** **worldview** was designed to **flatten** **borders** and
**boundaries** to **dismantle** **barriers** posed by regional laws and
national patent restrictions for pharmaceutical goods, as well as the
export of Western medical knowledge and expertise.5 Hence, the focus
shifted from one of civilizing underdeveloped nations to inte- grating
them within a global commercial exchange]{.underline}.

[However, when **nonhumans** or **subhumans**, as Yusoff (2018) argues,
traversed into the fold of a **universalized** **sense** **of**
**humanity**, enshrined in the idea of the global, the degree of their
humanness was again predicated on **hierarchies** **of** **worth**,
**power**, and **health**.]{.underline}6 Jingqiu Ren and Joe Feagin
(2021) suggest that [a **hierarchy** **of** **health** **persists**
**wherein** **those** **bodies** **considered** **healthy** **are**
**acceptable** **and** **those** **that** **are** **considered**
**weak** **and** **fallible** **are** **rejected** **by** **the**
**social** **order**. What is **considered** **weak** and **fallible**
**falls** **at** **the** **crossroads** **of** **numerous** **axes**
**of** **oppression** **such as age, sexuality, race, class, religion,
and (dis)ability**. Early twentieth-century eugenicists **explicitly
tied bodily weakness** and **frailty to poor genes** and **advocated for
the extinction of those they deemed not fit to survive.**]{.underline}
Later, in the period of nuclear testing and other kinds of medical
testing between 1945 and 1992 (Arms Control Association 2022), those not
fit to sur- vive became the guinea pigs, metaphorically and actually, of
scientific experiments tracking the impact of these tests.

#### The 1AC glorification of state management as a viable response to pandemics creates states of exceptions that justify surveillance, dictatorship, and increased racial management of populations. 

**Peters & Besley 20** (Michael A. Peters is a distinguished professor
of philosophy at the Beijing normal school; Tina Besley is a visiting
professor at Tsinghua university, "Biopolitics, conspiracy and the
immuno-state, October 6, 2020,
https://www.taylorfrancis.com/chapters/edit/10.4324/
9781003119579-11/biopolitics-conspiracy-immuno-state-michael-peters-tina-besley)

d 'The Invention of An Epidemic': Agamben (2020) endorses in the way
COVID-19 has enabled the tendency to use a state of exception as a
normal paradigm for government. He argues:

[Faced with the frenetic, irrational and entirely unfounded emergency
measures adopted against an alleged epidemic of coronavirus . . . why do
the media and the authorities do their utmost to spread a state of
panic, thus provoking an authentic state of exception with serious
limitations on movement and a suspension of daily life in entire
regions]{.underline}?

Agamben is concerned about 'the growing tendency to use the state of
exception as a normal paradigm of government' where the law has imposed
serious limitations on freedom, and he lists the following:

[(a) prohibition on the removal from the municipality or area concerned
by all individuals present in the municipality or area;\
(b) prohibition of access to the municipality or area concerned;\
(c) suspension of events or initiatives of any kind, of events and of
any form of meeting in a public or private place, including cultural,
recreational, sporting and religious, even if held in closed places open
to the public;\
(d) suspension of educational services for children and schools of all
levels, as well as the frequency of school and higher education
activities, except for distance learning activities;\
(e) suspension of the opening services to the public of museums and
other cultural institutes and places . . .;\
(f) suspension of all educational trips, both on national and foreign
territory;\
(g) suspension of bankruptcy proceedings and of the activities of public
offices, without prejudice to the provision of essential and public
utility services;\
(h) application of the quarantine measure with active surveillance among
individuals who have had close contacts with confirmed cases of diffuse
infectious disease. . . .]{.underline}

It would seem that once terrorism is exhausted as a cause for
exceptional measures, the invention of an epidemic could offer the ideal
pretext to extend them beyond all limits. . . . In a perverse vicious
circle, the limitation of freedom imposed by governments is accepted in
the name of a desire for security that has been prompted by the
governments themselves who now intervene to satisfy it.

e 'A biopolitical dream': Philipp Sarasin (2020), commenting on
Foucault's biopolitics in order to understand the pandemic, sums up the
apogee of the modern conception of politics constituted in terms of the
biological:

[It looks like a biopolitical dream: governments, advised by physicians,
impose pandemic dictatorship on entire populations. Getting rid of all
democratic obstacles under the pretext of 'health,' even 'survival,'
they are finally able to govern the population as they have, more or
less openly, always done in modernity: as pure 'biomass,' as 'bare life'
to be exploited.]{.underline}

He indicates three models by which modern societies have regulated
populations during pandemic events: first, 'The complete lockdown of
Wuhan rigorously follows the plague model, and every curfew ultimately
does so, too'; second, the liberal smallpox model of power---'The
strategy to #flattenthecurve means to reckon with the pathogen and to
know that it cannot be eradicated, but to "extend" its distribution over
time in such a way that the health system can handle it'; third, the old
leprosy model 'is lurking in the background . . . let old people die "to
save the economy"'---or abandon retirement and nursing homes.

In terms of COVID-19 we can significantly add to the analysis of
Foucault-Agamben line by acknowledging a political rationality for the
quarantine and social distancing of the pandemic that seems to suggest a
common theme: the 'quarantined subject' and 'staying at home'. This is
simultaneously a kind of social isolation at the physical level but
[reunification at the level of the command state and nation through the
spectacle of the media, including daily briefings, that asks individuals
and households to willingly curtail their freedoms in the interests of
the popular imaginary of a resilient and caring society.]{.underline}
[The crucial difference is that there is no camp but rather ordinary
life is turned inward on itself with enforced police and neighbourhood
surveillance]{.underline}. One problem with both Agamben and Foucault
(to a lesser extent) is that the analysis is unrelentingly the dark side
that connects the logic of the biopolitical to a state where the
elimination of an ethnic minority in the name of national unity bypasses
constitutional questions and sovereignty to lead us to a one-way
historical street. By contrast to Foucault and Agamben, Roberto Esposito
offers an affirmative bio-politics.

f Roberto Esposito: 'Bios, Immunity, Life'

Roberto Esposito is an Italian philosopher, a professor of theoretical
philosophy at the Scuola Normale Superiore, and one of the founders of
the European Political Lexicon Research Centre and the International
Centre for a European Legal and Political Lexicon. He is an important
philosopher, alongside Giorgio Agamben and Antonio Negri, in theorizing
biopolitics over the last 20 years, especially the origin and destiny of
community in relation to what he calls the 'The Immunization Paradigm',
exploring immunization as a discourse and developing an affirmative
biopolitics based on the three concepts included in the titles of his
trilogy: *Communitas* (2004, orig. 1998), *Immunitas* (2011, orig. 2002)
and *Bios* (2008).

In the essay 'Epidemic Paradoxia: Treated to the Bitter End' for
*Antinomies*, Roberto Esposito (2020) writes:

The fact is that today anyone with eyes to see cannot deny the full
deployment of biopolitics. From biotechnology interventions on areas
once considered exclusively natural such as birth and death, to
biological terrorism, to the management of immigration and more or less
serious epidemics, all current political conflicts have at the center
the relationship between politics and biological life.

Esposito argues that politics and medicine have become mutually linked
and transforming, including both 'a politicization of medicine' and 'a
medicalization of politics', creating a politics very different from its
classic role and profile. Commenting on Italy under the virus,
nevertheless, Esposito suggests that limitations of freedom 'has more
the character of a decomposition of public powers than of a dramatic
totalitarian grip'.

Esposito's work over the last 20 years has explored immunization as
discourse of the self and state and how both are constructed in relation
to foreign bodies with the potential for conflict. The sources for
Esposito's 'Affirmative Biopolitics' are to be found in Mauss
(gift-exchange), Hobbes' social contract, Locke, and Bataille's concept
of sacrifice (Tierney, 2016). His major work, *Immunitas: The Protection
and Negation of Life*, examines the relations between bodies and
societies and argues that 'just as the human body's immune system
protects the organism from deadly incursions by viruses and other
threats, law also ensures the survival of the community in a
life-threatening situation', protecting and prolonging life. The major
paradox, is that 'like the individual body, the collective body can be
immunized from the perceived danger only by allowing a little of what
threatens it to enter its protective boundaries'. The nature of
immunization, Esposito argues, is at the heart of contemporary
biopolitics, characterizing both the nature of the self and society. The
paradox is that immunity functions to incorporate within the body
exactly that foreign elements and external entity that threaten its
existence.

g The Immune Self: A significant range of ontological and
epistemological studies have been focused on theme of 'the immune self'
as 'the central motif of biological identity in the context of
immunity', invoking immunological conceptions of selfhood, including
personal identity, self-image, constructivist and poststructuralist
notions of identity, as well as questions in ecology and evolutionary
biology. As Swiatczak and Tauber (2020) explain:

Immunology, from its earliest inception, has been concerned with
biological identity---its establishment and maintenance. In its original
iteration, immunity was conceived as that function that preserved the
integrity of the organism in terms of protecting and restoring its
individuality. Thus, immune functions testified to the persistence of a
stable, core identity defined in terms of its insularity and autonomy.
Indeed, [individuality undergirded the science from its inception, for
the defense against pathogens was framed by an attacked patient
(individual) pitted against alien others, the invaders.]{.underline}

A major shift of thinking has occurred as dominance of 'the immune self'
and individuality-based biology has given way to communal ecological
perspectives reflecting some larger changes in biology to recognize
environmental studies, eco-immunology and symbiotic ecosystems, leading
to fundamental insights challenging notions of individuality and
pathogenization. Early notions of autonomy as a theoretical foundation
for biology 'complemented Malthusian economics, liberal political
philosophy, and Comtian sociology to instantiate the autonomous body as
a political, social, economic and medical entity' and failed to
comprehend 'the dialectical relationship between the organism and its
environment' (Swiatczak & Tauber, 2020). By contrast, a number of
critical philosophers (Foucault, Agamben, Haraway, Esposito) have
dismantled the old view suggesting 'the "[immune self" appears as a
metaphor to model Western culture, by invoking "immunization" as an
explanatory model for understanding the core dynamics of post-industrial
societies']{.underline} (Swiatczak & Tauber, 2020). These [criticisms
also apply also to notions of the state---'the immuno-state'---that is
theorized on the basis of a dialectics of border and exchange, intrusion
and defence, within an ecological and dynamic systems conception of the
state within an increasingly interconnected and globalized planet,
helping to reconceptualize contemporary immunology through collectivist
models of molecular interaction.]{.underline}

\[The\] ecological orientation has inflected immunology from its earlier
insular conceptions of the organism to a dynamic interactive view that
highlights how life experience alters the immune response to external
challenges and opportunities. With this broadened perspective, the idea
of immunity extends beyond defense of an atomistic individual, to
include the mediation of the organism's ecological economy, both
defensive and assimilative. (Swiatczak & Tauber, 2020)

China as immune-state demonstrated the role of a strong socialist state
that could act quickly to provide social and geopolitical isolation of
neighbourhoods, towns and cities, as well as an enforcement regime.
(Generally Chinese people welcomed and understood the need for such
measures in the collective good.) By contrast the 'immuno-state' in the
US under the Trump regime has proved controversial, often setting itself
against experts and its own scientific advisors, creating an ongoing
fight with governors on safety measures and on 'opening up'. Trump
himself has added to the contradictory nature of advice to the American
people through his own example (e.g., not wearing a mask, championing
false anti- products) and through his briefings. He puts great emphasis
on a busno- technological fix of a vaccine. These two styles of
government---American capitalist vs. Chinese socialist (in all their
differences)---may also determine how successfully these countries 'open
up' and also capitalization of global market opportunities, including
buying distressed strategic companies.

2 Global governance, viral politics and government by conspiracy

a Pandemic I: The First Modern Pandemic: Bill Gates writes: 'The
coronavirus pandemic pits all of humanity against the virus. The damage
to health, wealth, and well-being has already been enormous. This is
like a world war, except in this case, we're all on the same side'. He
emphasizes global innovation and the production of a vaccine.
Immediately after Trump announced the freezing of funds to the WHO, Bill
Gates criticized the action and the Gates Foundation announced it was
increasing its emergency funding over and above the \$100 million global
response funding made in February by an additional \$150 million of
grant funding for diagnostics, therapeutics and vaccines.

b The global governance of health: Chong (2020) suggests that at the
international level, global governance is tied to the control of borders
and the security of the state that can threaten civil rights and the
erosion of civil liberties necessary for democracy:

Much of the public alarm triggered by the outbreak of the novel
coronavirus (COVID-19) is greatly bound up with the management of
cross-border security threats. COVID-19 resembles a 21st-century
medieval plague in terms of how little we understand its character and
how vulnerable we are to its effects. . . . The coronavirus crisis is
emblematic of the difficulty of constructively extending good governance
across borders. An effective response requires governments, medical
authorities and civil society to consider a new spatialisation of
threats to human security when making decisions.

Chong (2020) argues that the global biosecurity threat depends upon
information and intelligence sharing in real time, for the COVID-19
virus afflicts everyone irrespective of political allegiance or
nationality, and he suggests, 'The global north and global south must
find a common depoliticised platform to exchange relevant information
about the latest viral threats in much the same way cyber security firms
and national cyber agencies monitor digital viruses in real time'. So
far the very opposite has occurred, with the White House stepping up its
conspiracy campaign against China over the origin and distribution of
the virus, and against the WHO for its alleged mishandling of the
pandemic when its own mismanagement of the pandemic crisis has been
injurious to the American people and the viral crisis has been
politicized as a crucial factor in the coming US election.

c 'Government by conspiracy': The political rationality, based on a
state of exception where the US 'national emergency' permits new powers
to dispense billions, gave three trillion dollars---the historically
largest bail-out---to businesses and unemployment benefits to keep the
economy afloat when borders are closed and will remained closed for the
foreseeable future. Trump's border security territoriality seems to be
achieved in one set of health/economy measures that ruptures daily
economic life. Yet there are clear governmentality differences between
socialist China and neoliberal America in the philosophy of public
health management. After early suppression of information and cover-up
attempts, China acted swiftly and effectively to embrace a total
shutdown of Wuhan and Hubei that quarantined the outbreak. In the US by
contrast, management was interrupted by a denial of COVID-19, the
consequent lack of testing and a disregard for public safety with
attempts to prematurely open up the economy again. As a consequence, the
US's grim COVID-19 statistics have ballooned out to 1.5 million
confirmed cases of infection and nearly 100,000 deaths (May 18).

d The Constitutional Challenge to Lockdown: Increasingly, echoing the
president, people in strong Republican states are contesting lockdown by
citing their inalienable rights enshrined in the US Constitution. As
Geranios (2020), for instance, reports:

In deeply conservative eastern Washington state, a prominent state
lawmaker kicked out of his Republican Party caucus labels the
coronavirus as a foreign bio-weapon, accuses Marxists of using the
pandemic to advance totalitarianism and rails against lockdown
restrictions imposed by the Democratic governor. . . . Across the U.S.,
elected officials from Pennsylvania to Oklahoma suspicious of big
government and outraged with orders to close churches, gun stores and
other businesses deemed non-essential insist that the public health
response is being used as an excuse to trample constitutional rights.

The Far Right suggests that a national emergency has been used to limit
civil freedoms of citizens of the United States. Some are worried about
the State-enhanced surveillance powers and the control over information;
others express concern about the suspension of rights, especially
freedom of association. The 'Stay Home. Saves Lives' campaign's focus on
social distancing, quarantine and breaking the chain of transmission is
now an argument about the constitutionality of government actions.
Paradoxically, the challenge comes from both the Far Right and those
wishing to prevent a totalitarian state. Critics are concerned that the
COVID-19 government responses have been and are being used for ends that
are not related to the preservation of public health.

The confusion around lockdown limitations on freedoms in the US are
partly a result of the contradictions between the federal and state
levels of power, often determined by whether governors are Democrat or
Republican, and the extent to which they are tuned to the centre. This
structure of formal authority and power is made more complex by the fact
that President Trump contradicts his own official announcements and
policies (by tweeting opposing content). There is also often a clear
conflict between what Trump says at briefings and the advice of his
scientific-medical team. These Science---State conflicts relate to the
significance of epidemiological sciences of prediction and mathematical
modelling to estimate exponential rates of infection and
infection-deaths ratios, and the role they play in national emergency
policies.

There are also calls for 'opening up the economy' in the US that Trump
privately supports in his tweets to his followers while at the same time
stressing the importance of social distancing in public. President Trump
has unveiled Guidelines for Opening Up America Again, including
'downward trajectory of influenza-like illnesses (ILI) reported within a
14-day period', core State responsibilities in 'preparedness' in terms
of testing and contact-tracing, plans to protect workers and guidelines.
The moral choice of further deaths, especially of frontline workers, and
other trade-offs of economy and risks to health---a choice now left to
governors---has become a calculus that operates at the State level,
generating a significant level risk for all cross-border activities.

3 The immuno-state: biosafety, biosecurity, bioterrorism, biowarfare

a The concept of Biosafety Level as a set of precautions and containment
procedures to isolate dangerous pathogens in the laboratory originated
after World War II and took shape during the 1980s through a set of
conferences to develop a formal organization, The American Biological
Safety Association (ABSA), in 1984 and, later, four levels of biosafety
expressed as BSL1--4. *Biosafety in Microbiological and Biomedical
Laboratories*, first published in 1984 and now in its 5th edition
(Wilson & Chosewood, 2009), is an advisory document outlining best
practices. It provides guidance on biological risk assessment,
principles of biosafety including biosafety levels, laboratory safety
level criteria (specifying standard microbiological practices, special
practices, safety equipment and laboratory facilities), animal biosafety
level criteria and principles of laboratory biosecurity, as well as a
summary statement of microbiological agents. The Introduction makes
clear the risks associated with expansion of research and bioterrorism:

We are living in an era of uncertainty and change. New infectious agents
and diseases have emerged. Work with infectious agents in public and
private research, public health, clinical and diagnostic laboratories,
and in animal care facilities has expanded. Recent world events have
demonstrated new threats of bioterrorism. For these reasons,
organizations and laboratory directors are compelled to evaluate and
ensure the effectiveness of their biosafety programs, the proficiency of
their workers, as well as the capability of equipment, facilities, and
management practices to provide containment and security of
microbiological agents.

The occurrence of laboratory-associated infections seems limited, with
some 168 deaths recorded between 1930 and 1978, and 22 deaths in the
following 20 years. Risk levels evolved with BSL-4, defined in the
following terms: "Exotic agents that pose a high individual risk of
life-threatening disease by infectious aerosols and for which no
treatment is available are restricted to high containment laboratories
that meet biosafety level 4 (BSL-4) standards."

b The Centers for Disease Control and Prevention have more than 1,700
scientists working in some 200 laboratories across the US with the
appointment of one official responsible for overall laboratory safety.
BSL-4 laboratories, as the highest level, require work to be completed
within level II biosafety cabinets, with decontamination procedures,
restricted entry, the wearing of protective suits and chemical showers
on exit. Research takes place on easily transmitted pathogens that can
cause death, including Ebola, Marburg, Lassa, viral hemorrhagic fevers
(VHFs) and Variola virus (which causes smallpox) in two research
centres, one in the US and one in Russia.

c There are some 70 BSL-4 reported laboratories globally with 15 in the
US alone, although the number is not verified. It includes the Center
for Biodefense and Emerging Infectious Diseases that has specified BSL
levels. The WHO Consultative Meeting on High/Maximum Containment
(Biosafety Level 4) Laboratories Networking (2018) reports:

BSL-4 laboratories represent the highest level of biological
containment, offering unparalleled protection for the user, sample and
environment. At present, more than 50 maximum- and high-containment
facilities around the globe handle some of the world's most hazardous
pathogens to human and animal health for research and diagnostic
purposes. BSL4 laboratories are located in all WHO regions. While most
are in North America or western Europe, a number have been built in
Asia, and construction projects are underway in China, Japan and
sub-Saharan Africa, raising questions related to sustainability in
low-income countries.

There has been a shift from prescriptive to performance-based biosafety,
emphasizing "the use of practical measures to mitigate risks, including
thorough risk assessments and evidence-based approaches to biosafety,
rather than reliance on rigid classification systems." The meeting
document provided updates on BSL-4 facilities, including planned
high-containment laboratories (Japan, China, UK), high-containment
laboratories under construction (Côte d'Ivoire, US, UK, China), recently
constructed BSL-4 laboratories that are operational (China, Korea,
Australia, Germany), as well as high-containment laboratory networks.

d There have been fears about biosafety and human error rates with these
labs, as well as criticisms of the lack of government regulation in the
US. Alexandra Peters (2019) writes of "the global proliferation of
high-containment biological laboratories":

Disease-causing pathogens have been with humanity for as long as the
species has existed, but the world has changed. The human population is
increasing and becoming more globalised. Meanwhile, the international
system remains unstable and biotechnology is advancing at a breakneck
speed. Humans are coming into contact with new and re-emerging pathogens
as they spread into previously uninhabited environments. Pathogens play
an increasingly global role, and infectious disease is becoming less
confined by geographical or climatic boundaries. In order to meet these
new challenges, both states and the private sector have been building an
increasing number of high-containment biological laboratories (HCBLs)
that work with biosafety level (BSL) 3 and 4 pathogens. This rate has
increased sharply since 11 September 2011, and most states that have the
means to build such laboratories do so. Pathogens do not stop at
borders, and the more prepared a state is to deal with them, the better
for its national security.

In a case study of the US, Peters indicates that there has been a rapid
proliferation of HCBLs and issues to do with the lack of oversight and
reporting with discrepancies in reporting and threats posed by accidents
at government facilities, including the mishandling of anthrax. In terms
of future challenges there are threats of both biological weapons and
bioterrorism. While there has been some progress in terms of better
oversight of HCBLs and an increase in international cooperation, "the
measures that have been taken so far are not yet adequate, and the
degree of oversight and control that states exert over their HCBLs
varies considerably."

e The COVID-19 virus became politicized early on, as Trump by turn first
praised China's handling of the crisis, then as the situation got worse,
pointed the finger and blamed, in order, the media, the governors, the
WHO (freezing funding) and the "Chinese virus", drawing attention to the
Wuhan Lab and deflecting responsibility for the crisis---what we call
"government by conspiracy." The Wuhan Lab conspiracy was supported by
Trump, Pence and Pompeo, but contradicted by their own intelligence and
health advisors like Anthony Fauci, who echoed the scientific consensus
that the COVID-19 virus was "natural" and showed no signs of being
genetically engineered.

f Biosecurity: The concept and preventive measures of biosecurity
developed quickly in the 1990s in relation to the increasing
interconnectivity of globalization to prevent the transmission of
infectious crop and animal diseases that could damage people, the
environment and agrobusinesses. Later it was extended to include
modified organisms (GMOs) and invasive alien species. The first GMO
plants and animals date from the 1970s but it was not until the 1990s
that the first commercial genetically modified food began production.
Bacteria are the easiest to modify and they have been used in research
and food production. Viruses also are modified through transduction
(inserting genetic material), especially in gene therapy. In the 1980s
biosecurity was extended to preventing forms of bioterrorism. Most
recently, biosecurity has been extended again to cope with the growing
exigencies of synthetic biology. The movement toward securitization at
the international level came to include a number of other risks
including generalized public health risks to entire populations, such as
epidemics and pandemics. Increasingly, states within the international
system looked to control malevolent uses of infectious biological
diseases by state and non-state actors. As Koblentz (2010) argues:

Advances in science and technology, the rise of globalization, the
emergence of new diseases, and the changing nature of conflict have
increased the risks posed by naturally occurring and man-made biological
threats. A growing acceptance of a broader definition of security since
the end of the Cold War has facilitated the rise of biosecurity issues
on the international security agenda.

Biosecurity has come to encompass a wide range of biological threats
that include natural, accidental and deliberate disease outbreaks, and
states now need a comprehensive framework to enforce border security
increasingly at the viral level.

g The risks of bioterrorism: Bioterrorism using micro-organisms or
infected samples to cause terror and panic in populations is an ancient
form. Hittites sent infected rams to their enemies and the British in
1763 reportedly gave indigenous people blankets infected with smallpox.
In the modern context these threats have been highlighted following the
World Trade Center attack of September 2001 with the "anthrax" scare
(FBI code Amerithax) where spores were mailed to Democratic senators,
killing five people and infecting 17. Already in the COVID-19 pandemic,
White supremacists who have tested positive have been encouraged to
infect Jews and cops.

h Biowarfare and bioweapons: The Biological Weapons Convention (BWC,
1972) was the first international treaty to effectively prohibit an
entire category of weapons of mass destruction. It has been confirmed by
183 states, including the US and UK. The BWC bans the development,
stockpiling, acquisition, retention and production of (i) biological
agents and toxins "of types and in quantities that have no justification
for prophylactic, protective or other peaceful purposes" and (ii)
weapons, equipment and delivery vehicles "designed to use such agents or
toxins for hostile purposes or in armed conflict." Despite the
international treaty, it has failed to stop countries from conducting
offensive weapons research and large-scale production of biological
weapons. The BWC has no inspection or verification procedures and while
only a few countries are known to have bioweapons programs, the
development of bioweapons compared to nuclear weapons are inexpensive
and both easy to acquire and to hide behind benign research
institutions. To add to these difficulties, much of the information
concerning the production of harmful pathogens are available on the
Internet.

i\
The immuno-state faces increasing levels of risk from globalization and
the transmission of harmful viruses, bacteria and infectious diseases
across international borders that are difficult to detect and police,
especially air-borne and ocean-borne agents. The threat of biological
weapons seems weak. The argument has been that rational state agents are
unlikely to use this means of warfare given the risks of blow-back and
control issues. Natural and accidental pathogens are more likely risks,
and threats to the environment and to agriculture also represent a
greater cause for concern. Modern immunology since its inception has
used a philosophical language consisting of "self" and "nonself" or
"self/foreign." Such language has given way to community and ecological
models that recognize mutualism and symbiotic relationships between the
organism and the environment. [The state as an organism is part of an
underlying political environment that seeks to manage the macro immune
system. In classical politics the metaphor of the natural body to the
body politic operated on a metaphor that compared diseases to political
afflictions (civil conflict, dissent, resistance). The body politic has
passed from a sovereignty of death to a body politic to preserve life
and health of the population. The interpretation of the social in
biological terms has led to Social Darwinism and the racial state
governed by a notion of population hygiene that has been adopted and
refined recently by the Far Right to fuel contemporary notions of
nationalism and populism.]{.underline}

### L---Extinction

#### Utilitarianism's prioritization of potential human life over present suffering is [indebted]{.underline} to the logic of extinctiopolitics---a necropolitical decision-making calculus built on the hierarchical ordering of speculative risk. 

Joe P.L. **Davidson 22**, Leverhulme Early Career Fellow in the
Department of Sociology at the University of Warwick, PhD in the
Department of Sociology at the University of Cambridge, September 2022,
"Extinctiopolitics: Existential Risk Studies, the Extinctiopolitical
Unconscious, and the Billionaires' Exodus From Earth," *New Formations:
a journal of culture/theory/politics*, Issue 107-8, p. 48-65, Project
*MUSE*, https://muse.jhu.edu/article/881492) DJDubb

[The futural perspective of **extinctiopolitics**]{.underline} has
several important effects. Biopolitics [**is** defined by **a
utilitarian logic**]{.underline}; it is [**concerned with**]{.underline}
maximising utility across the population. Indeed, on the biopolitical
perspective, the vigour of one part of the population may be dependent
on the demise of another part of the population. As Mbembe emphasises,
biopolitics involves [the zero-sum game of **deciding**
']{.underline}who is able to live and **[who must die']{.underline}**;
certain people die [(especially those in the 'death-worlds' of the
colonial zone) **so that others survive**]{.underline} (Necropolitics,
p66, p92, emphasis in original). This logic is mirrored by
extinctiopolitics, which is also concerned with maximising utility.
However, on the 'total [utilitarianism]{.underline}' advanced by the
field, utility [refers **not only** to **people** alive **in the present
but all** the **potential humans** that could be alive at any point in
time]{.underline}.24 As Ord puts it: 'Our lives matter just as much as
those lived thousands of years ago, or those a thousand years hence'
(The Precipice, p28). The biopolitical task of 'distributing the living
in the domain of value and utility' is thus understood in terms of the
life of the species as a whole. 25 The expansive utilitarianism of
existential risk studies means that the future has a strong hold on the
present. Given that a minuscule portion of the total number of humans
that could exist are alive in the present moment, there is a strong
compulsion to act in favour of the many billions of potential people
that may exist in the future. Reducing existential risk is an especially
pressing priority; if humanity is extinguished, these potential lives
will be lost forever. [**The infinite mass of hypothetical people** in
the future **is granted a privileged position over** the handful of
**people** alive **in the present**.]{.underline} Extinctiopolitics
involves the 'power to foster life or disallow it to the point of
death'; [people in **the present** may be **sacrificed to increase
utility in the future**]{.underline} (History of Sexuality, p138).

If the negative side of biopolitics involves the utilitarian logic of
distributing life and death, then the positive side of biopolitics
involves the improvement and strengthening of the population.
Existential risk studies shares this biopolitical utopianism, but it
understands it as something that can only be achieved in the very
distant future. Andrew Leigh, largely drawing on Bostrom and Ord,
suggests that humanity is capable of building a 'heavenly paradise' ...
'this future is probable if humans can survive for another thousand
years. And it is almost certain if humans can survive for ten thousand
years'.26 More specifically, in common with biopolitics, **[there is a
quasi-eugenicist current to this image of the future]{.underline}**,
with Bostrom declaring in his transhumanist 'Letter from Utopia' (2008)
[that humanity will be substantially different at an ontological level
in the future]{.underline}.27 For instance, Bostrom predicts that, in
his utopia, life expectancy will be greater than 500 years and the
majority of the population will have 'cognitive capacities more than two
standard deviations above the current human maximum'.28

The future of humanity, as imagined by Bostrom and Ord, is dependent on
one very important factor: avoiding existential risk. If the normative
dimension of existential risk studies involves a utilitarian claim about
future possibility, then the empirical dimension of the field focuses on
identifying the most salient dangers to humanity. Like biopolitics more
generally, [**there is a keen concern with the calculation of risk** in
extinctiopolitics;]{.underline} both are concerned with [aggregating
information to predict, prevent, and regulate threats.]{.underline}29
Indeed, [there is an actuarial logic to]{.underline} existential risk
studies; thinkers associated with the movement are concerned with
[assigning probabilities to catastrophic dangers]{.underline}. Yet,
certain risks are much more significant than others for
extinctiopolitics. The risks that ultimately matter are those that have
the capacity to cause human extinction or, at very least, 'permanently
and drastically curtail \[humanity's\] potential' in the long-term
future (Existential Risk, p5). In Bostrom's words, other forms of risk,
even ones that cause widespread death such as genocides and world wars,
are 'mere ripples on the surface of the great sea of life' (Existential
Risk, p5). The task of existential risk studies is to calculate a
probability for an unprecedented event: human extinction. There is a
general agreement in the field that it is unlikely that human extinction
will occur from natural causes (see The Precipice, p92). Instead, the
real threats stem from the actions of humanity itself. With some
consistency, scholars associated with existential risk studies pinpoint
artificial intelligence as the key threat to humanity
(Superintelligence; The Precipice). Misaligned artificial intelligence,
or superintelligent machines that are indifferent to human life, is
granted huge destructive potential. On Ord's quantification of the
existential risk landscape, there is a one in ten risk that artificial
intelligence will cause the extinction of humanity or severely undermine
its long-term potential in the next century (The Precipice, p81).

With **[this attempt to calculate]{.underline}** the probabilities of
[different **risks and arrange them in a hierarchy of
seriousness,**]{.underline} existential risk studies
**[reinforces]{.underline}** what Frédéric Neyrat calls [the
'**biopolitics** of catastrophe']{.underline}, the distinctive mark of
[which is a 'continuous exacerbated sensitivity to
risks']{.underline}.30 Extinctiopolitics posits that existential risk is
the key question of our time; we are living in an especially critical
moment of human history. Humanity, over the last century, has acquired
the technological power to annihilate itself but lacks the moral and
political resources required to control this new destructive capacity.
As Bostrom stresses: '...our species is introducing entirely new kinds
of existential risk -- threats we have no track record of surviving'.31
Ord suggests that the current era of history, which he terms 'the
Precipice', is defined by 'the highest levels of risk' (The Precipice,
p23). Either humanity will succeed in mastering the instruments of
annihilation and build a transhuman future or it will succumb to them
and lose this potential for paradise forever.

Echoing Rosa Luxemburg's famous declaration of socialism or barbarism,
existential risk studies claims that the options are utopia or
extinction. The starkness of this choice shapes the concrete political
changes advocated by the field. Many of the [**reforms**]{.underline}
advanced by scholars [associated with]{.underline} existential risk are
relatively constrained. In contrast to Luxemburg, **[preventing
extinction]{.underline}** and realising utopia [**will not require** the
fundamental **transformation of social relations**]{.underline}.
Instead, [they propose greater funding for bodies concerned with
preventing global catastrophe]{.underline}. For instance, Ord notes that
the 'international body responsible for the continued prohibition of
bioweapons (the Biological Weapons Convention) has an annual budget of
just \$1.4 million -- less than the average McDonald's restaurant' (The
Precipice, p33). Furthermore, there is a close association between
existential risk studies and the effective altruism movement, which
instructs its followers to choose careers that do good in the world or
donate a percentage of their earnings to charitable causes (Ripples,
pp16-22). In a similar fashion, Ord encourages those concerned about
extinction to become artificial intelligence safety researchers or
donate some of their wages to support research into existential risk
(The Precipice, pp102-103). These strategies are symptomatic of the
emergent status of extinctiopolitics as a form of biopolitics. If
biopolitics has resulted in the establishment of academic disciplines
and government departments, the epistemological and institutional
resources of extinctiopolitics are much more limited.

However, existential risk studies has larger ambitions than guiding
career choices and boosting the funding of research institutions. If,
for Foucault, biopolitics' 'technology of security' aims at 'achieving
an overall equilibrium that protects the security of the whole from
internal dangers', then the technology of security advocated by
extinctiopolitics is more dynamic; it is concerned with achieving
security via perfection rather than equilibrium (Society Must be
Defended, p249). As Schuster and Woods note, for Bostrom, 'it would be
an existential risk to not pursue utopia' (Calamity Theory, p34,
emphasis in original). The threats faced by humanity can be defused
through a 'sustainable trajectory' of technological development
(Existential Risk Prevention, p26). New technologies are double-sided:
they increase existential risk but can also be used to safeguard the
future of humanity. For example, spreading out into the cosmos, to use
the actuarial logic of extinctiopolitics, offers an insurance option
against the extinction of humanity. Bostrom stresses that the 'failure
to colonise \[space\]' will mean that humanity is more likely to 'fall
victim to an existential risk'.32 As such, a pressing priority of the
contemporary moment is to advance aerospace technologies: 'For every
year that development of such technologies and colonisation of the
universe is delayed, there is therefore a corresponding opportunity
cost: a potential good, lives worth living, is not being realised'
(Astronomical Waste, p308). With comments like these, the appeal of
existential risk studies to space entrepreneurs like Musk becomes clear:
without the billionaire exodus from the Earth, doom is more likely.

WHAT DOES EXTINCTIOPOLITICS DO?

In the previous section, I refrained from critically analysing
extinctiopolitics. However, even from a cursory overview of the field of
existential risk studies, serious limitations are apparent. Building on
the critical accounts of the field published in recent years, several
key points should be highlighted. Normatively speaking, the total
**[utilitarianism]{.underline}** advanced by Bostrom and Ord [appears to
**prioritise** the **survival** of the human species **over** the
**wellbeing** of actually existing people]{.underline} (The Dangerous
Ideas; Democratising Risk). Karin Kuhlemann, adapting Parfit's example
above, instructively contrasts two possible scenarios: one in which 'a
global catastrophe causes ninety-nine per cent of the human population
to slowly starve to death' and the second in which 'the human lineage
gently dwindles into extinction without any suffering (e.g. because
individuals lose interest in procreation)'.33 On the logic of
existential risk studies, the former scenario is preferable because,
despite causing a huge amount of suffering to living people, it
safeguards the future of the species. [The **utilitarian** logic of
**extinctiopolitics sidelines** the **suffering** of actual people
**and**, worse, **could be used to justify inflicting suffering** in the
name of protecting the future of humanity]{.underline} (Calamity Theory,
p24, p52).34 If it is the case that space colonisation is one of the
best strategies for protecting the future of the species, [any
**exploitation and oppression**]{.underline} associated with initiatives
like Musk's SpaceX [**are legitimate**; they are **a small price to be
paid for reducing existential risk.**]{.underline}

The empirical account of the threats facing humanity can also be
questioned. In particular, as discussed further below, since there is
only a small possibility that climate change will totally eliminate
humanity, it is deprioritised by philosophers associated with
existential risk (see The Dangerous Ideas). Furthermore, [while there is
a particular focus on the risks]{.underline} posed by technology, such
as nuclear weapons, synthetic biology and artificial intelligence,
[there is **very little attention to**]{.underline} the [**social and
political structures**]{.underline} that shape these technologies and
how they are used.35 [Systematic d**iscussions of capitalism,
colonialism, racism, patriarchy**]{.underline} and so on **[are entirely
absent]{.underline}** in the writings of Bostrom and Ord. Moreover, [the
field obscures the differential effects of catastrophes; **the**
combined and **uneven consequences of extinction** across space and time
**are flattened**.]{.underline} For instance, [there is **no
acknowledgement that**, **for many** Indigenous peoples, **the end of
the world** is not something for the future but, instead, an event that
**has already happened**]{.underline} (I return to this point in the
final section).

Finally, it is unclear whether the solutions posited by existential risk
studies are adequate. Bostrom and Ord, against Günther Anders'
pessimistic claim in the face of nuclear disaster that 'the possibility
of our self-extinction cannot end -- except through the end itself ',
assert that humanity will eventually escape the age of existential
risk.36 In the utopian world imagined, preventing extinction is no
longer a priority; existential risk will have been conquered. However,
as others stress, the utopian ambitions of the field may increase rather
than reduce existential risk.37 For example, the advanced technologies
required for space colonisation could be easily refunctioned for
destructive ends and trigger what Daniel Deudney calls an 'astrocide',
or 'the extinction of humanity caused directly or indirectly by
largescale human space expansion' (Dark Skies, p362). Moreover, just as
the social causes of catastrophe are obscured, the social relations of
the future utopia are vaguely described; it appears that the future
world of liberation will leave the structures of the present untouched.
Indeed, the positive content of the transhumanist utopia imagined by
Bostrom reflects and reinforces social inequalities. As Claire Colebrook
notes, the lionisation of cognitive activity as the sine qua non of the
future world is 'bound up with the "man" of Western reason', a figure
that is strongly associated with many forms of oppression and violence
(especially in relation to race, disability, gender and sexuality)
(Lives Worth Living, p167).

In the light of the dubious normative consequences, skewed empirical
account of the threats to humanity and flawed vision of the liberated
future, it is difficult to affirm extinctiopolitics. However, these
criticisms do not examine why extinctiopolitics has emerged in the
current conjuncture. What is the relationship between, on the one hand,
thinking the future in terms of threats to human extinction and, on the
other, the social struggles, political crises and cultural anxieties of
the last two decades? What is it about existential risk studies that
resonates with philosophers at prestigious universities and billionaires
in the tech industry? As David Sepkoski argues more generally, there is
a close association between, on the one hand, discourses of extinction
and, on the other, the social and political context in which they are
elaborated.38 For instance, the theory that the dinosaurs were
annihilated by an asteroid that triggered huge climatic changes, which
was first proposed in the 1980s, can be understood in terms of broader
Cold War anxieties about the threat of a nuclear winter. The past end of
the dinosaurs and the future end of humanity were bound together by
widespread fascination with 'sudden, cataclysmic events capable of
obliterating life in the flash of an atomic blast or an asteroid impact'
(Catastrophic Thinking, p298). Given this, are there similar resonances
at play in the popularity of existential risk studies in the
contemporary moment?

It should first be stressed that this is a subversive question to pose
to extinctiopolitics. For existential risk studies, we need to abstract
ourselves from the immediate concerns of the contemporary moment. [The
object of extinctiopolitics is the long-term trajectory of
humanity]{.underline}; it traces the entire history of the species from
its origins to its multiplanetary and transhumanist future. From this
perspective, [the **political and social concerns** of the present
moment,]{.underline} especially those [that **are deemed**
non-existential]{.underline} in their stakes, [are
**insignificant.**]{.underline} For instance, Leigh encourages us to
place ourselves in the grand arc of human history: 'Suppose humanity's
potential time on the planet was shrunk down to a single eighty-year
life span. In that event, we would now be a newborn baby -- just nine
days old' (What's the Worst, p10). Indeed, scholars associated with
existential risk studies voice frustration with politicians for their
short-term perspective; they respond to today's headlines rather than
the long-term interests of the species. Ord recounts that: 'When I have
raised the topic of existential risk with senior politicians and civil
servants, I have encountered a common reaction: genuine deep concern
paired with a feeling that addressing the greatest risks facing humanity
was "above my pay grade"' (The Precipice, p35). Similarly, William
MacAskill, another proponent of existential risk studies, recalls the
response when he talked about future pandemics at a meeting with Nicola
Sturgeon, the First Minister of Scotland, in 2017: 'Everyone laughed,
and the host of the dinner, Sir Tom Hunter, joked that I was "freaking
everyone oot"'.39

However, [**the disavowals of presentism** amongst the proponents of
existential risk studies **should not be taken at face value**. There
are resonances between extinctiopolitics and the conjuncture in which it
emerged;]{.underline} it is entangled with a set of concerns that are
specific to the moment of the 2000s and 2010s. [**Extinctiopolitics
chimes with the broader feeling of crisis and catastrophe** that has
emerged since the turn of the millennium.]{.underline} It is perhaps no
accident that 2002 witnessed the publication of Bostrom's foundational
paper on existential risk and the establishment of Musk's aerospace
company SpaceX. The terrorist attacks of 11 September 2001, as Phillip
E. Wegner notes, marked the decisive end of the long 1990s in the
American context.40 Like the extinction risks highlighted by Bostrom,
the event of 9/11 inaugurated a new understanding of the future in which
it is defined not by endless peace and prosperity but instead decisive
caesuras and disastrous failures. Furthermore, [the **catastrophic
consciousness** fostered by existential risk studies **has found succour
in a range of events** in the decades that have followed **9/11,**
including **the global financial crisis of 2007/08,** **the rise of
authoritarian populist governments** across Europe and North America in
the 2010s, the **COVID-19** pandemic that began in 2020 **and the
Russian invasion of Ukraine** in 2022.]{.underline} However,
[**extinctiopolitics has a particular affinity with the apocalyptic mood
that surrounds the current climate crisis**,]{.underline} something
captured by Greta Thunberg's declaration that the world is on fire and
Extinction Rebellion's claim that we are living in a climate
emergency.41 The 'Anthropocene is the Apocalypse', its arrival marking
the end of the relative stability of the Holocene era and a beginning of
a new world of radical ruptures and unprecedented events.42 In this
context, images of human extinction become convincing. In the face of
the revenge of Gaia, what hope is there for humanity?

### L---Extractivism

#### Extractivism operates through the logic of necrocapitalism: it racializes, dispossesses, and expels Indigenous communities while masking violence behind liberal development narratives. Extractive infrastructures mark Indigenous land and life as expendable to capitalist accumulation, producing "death-worlds" under the guise of progress, law, and democracy. Even when Indigenous voices are "included," their consent is instrumentalized to legitimate dispossession. The state no longer needs to kill directly\--it simply lets Indigenous life decay under state-sanctioned petro-development.

**Rivera 21** \[Ángel Eduardo Rivera. Rivera is a part of the
International Relations Center. He's a faculty member of Political and
Social Sciences (FCPS) at the National Autonomous University of Mexico
(UNAM) in Mexico City, Mexico. 6-26-2021. Chapter 7. "Contested
Necrocapitalism: Indigeneity Versus Extractivism in Northern Canada."
Necropower in North America. Palgrave Macmillan, Cham. pp 129--150.
https://doi-org.proxy.lib.umich.edu/10.1007/978-3-030-73659-0_7 \] //ins
