#### The state is a [process]{.underline} that must be [continually engaged]{.underline} in addition to working outside of it. Violence does not [inhere]{.underline} in the state, BUT [abandoning]{.underline} the state cedes it to conservative capture and means the alt cannot [scale]{.underline} to [governance]{.underline} problems associated with ecological justice.

Jill Lindsey **HARRISON** Geography @ UC Boulder **'22** "Environmental
Justice and the State" *EPE: Nature and Space* Forthcoming p. 7-15

The prospects for EJ through the state

Additionally, these recent critical assessments raise serious doubts
about whether EJ movements should engage with the state to help redress
environmental injustice. They do so in part by specifying how states'
responses to EJ movements have been deeply disappointing, entailing
small concessions that fall short of, coopt, and neuter activists'
demands. For instance, [[Pulido]{.underline}]{.mark} and colleagues
[[list]{.mark} the [failures of]{.mark} "key avenues in which
[EJ]{.mark} activists have sought [relief from the
state]{.mark}"]{.underline} (Pulido, 2017a: 525): filing lawsuits
against government agencies for violations of the Equal Protection
clause of the U.S. Constitution's 14th amendment, filing administrative
complaints against agencies for violating Title VI of the Civil Rights
Act, advising agencies on how to implement EO 12898, and pressuring
agencies to enforce environmental law more stringently in overburdened
communities (see also Pulido, 2016, 2018; Pulido et al., 2016).
Similarly, Pellow argues that EJ movements have been "ultimately
achieving relatively little by way of state changes," noting the U.S.
EPA's minimal progress on implementing EO 12898 and how few remedies
overburdened and marginalized communities have received through the
legal system. These regulatory shortcomings certainly warrant
condemnation, and other scholars have rightly criticized agencies' EJ
policy implementation efforts for insufficiently redressing
environmental injustice (Harrison, 2019; Holifield, 2004, 2012; Konisky,
2015; Liévanos, 2012; London et al., 2008; Shilling et al., 2009).

Yet their critiques of the state are not limited to specific failures
but are more expansive and rooted in critical theories of the state.
[Drawing on]{.underline} recent [scholarship on racial capitalism,
settler colonialism, enslavement, and carcerality]{.underline} that
provide crucial counterpoints to pluralist, uncritical treatments of the
state, [they argue that states produce racial hierarchies, environmental
inequalities, and other forms of ideological and material violence in
the service of capital accumulation and colonial
dispossession]{.underline}.2 Citing seminal work on racial capitalism by
Cedric Robinson and subsequent engagements with it by Ruth Wilson
Gilmore (2002) and others, Pulido (2016) explains that the "devaluation
of Black (and other nonwhite) bodies has been a central feature of
global capitalism for centuries (Robinson, 2000) and creates a landscape
of differential value which can be harnessed in diverse ways to
facilitate the accumulation of more power and profit than would
otherwise be possible" (1). Moreover, the state "refuses to implement
meaningful initiatives" to support EJ because environmental inequality
is inherent to and central to the state's role in fostering capitalism:
"environmental racism is constituent of racial capitalism" (Pulido,
2017a: 529, 524). "The state is not about to dismantle this 'ecological
service' that allows firms to remain competitive in the global
marketplace" (Pulido, 2017a: 529).

Pellow (2014, 2016, 2018, 2021) and Kojola and Pellow (2021) also draw
on critical race theory, as well as scholarship from anarchist studies,
intersectional feminism, and other fields, to argue that environmental
inequalities are not accidental aberrations but one of the many forms of
hierarchy that the state necessarily creates and maintains through
violence to foster capital accumulation and control. [[Pellow
argues]{.mark} that]{.underline} these [forms of [violence "are
foundational to]{.mark}]{.underline} the formation and [[maintenance of
states]{.underline}]{.mark} and capital and that therefore it would
likely be counterproductive for environmental justice scholars and
advocates to seek solutions from dominant institutions"---that we should
not "expect the very institutions that are largely driving
socioecological inequalities to offer remedies for these harms" (2018:
151).

Pellow, Pulido, and colleagues have played leading roles in bringing
critical social theory into EJ studies in ways that push the field in
important new directions. They emphasize that capitalism does not only
produce racial inequality but actually relies on it, that the state
actively and foundationally creates and fosters racial hierarchies, and
that those hierarchies of value are not accidental but instead are a
productive, central part of the functioning of capitalism. Their
scholarship serves as crucial correctives to much environmental
inequality research that treats race simply as a demographic variable
and as functioning independently of the state and the market. Pulido's
work in particular details how racial systems have changed over time in
response to social movement challenges and in ways that naturalize and
otherwise shore up the status quo. Moreover, this scholarship, and
subsequent studies it has helped inform and inspire, document a vast
array of ways that state institutions participate in these processes.
Pellow's work is especially powerful in this regard, as he connects a
wide array of examples, some not usually seen as "environmental," to
show how pervasively the state constructs and reinforces status
hierarchies through violence and that this violence takes many
intersecting forms (including racism, classism, anthropocentrism,
sexism, ableism, etc.).

These publications counsel EJ scholars and activists to not just
decenter the state but turn away from it, arguing that engaging with the
state is counterproductive and perpetuates injustice. Pellow emphasizes
that much can be gained through practicing direct democracy without
waiting for the state or playing by the rules it sets. He offers brief
examples of activists collectively pursuing more just worlds beyond the
state: grassroots, horizontally organized groups providing food and
shelter, cleaning up contaminated sites, and engaging in other mutual
aid (2018: 25). Pellow acknowledges that such enactments of direct
democracy are prone to the oppression and domination that pervade
broader society. Hence, he challenges EJ scholars and activists to
interrogate how hierarchies of value---including white supremacy,
patriarchy, cis-heteronormativity, and speciesism, and, crucially, their
intersections---manifest within spaces of not only mainstream but also
radical environmental politics (Pellow, 2014, 2018).

Like Pellow, Pulido and de Lara (2018) argue for pursuing change beyond
the state. [They advocate for EJ scholars and activists to build and
foster different kinds of relationships]{.underline}, consciousness,
forms of political mobilization, and ways of being that enable us to
imagine a "form of freedom that does not revert to nation-bound
definitions of peoplehood and nature" and are not embedded within "the
same racial and capitalist relationships of power that produced the
colonial project of plunder and dispossession in the Americas" (Pulido
and de Lara, 2018: 78).

To be sure, their recommendations entail some complexity. Notably,
Pulido and colleagues emphasize that EJ activists and scholars must
expect the state to serve capital accumulation, not justice, and thus
must approach the state through confrontation. For instance, Pulido
argues that "EJ activists and researchers should view the state as a
site of contestation, rather than as an ally or neutral force" (2017a:
524) and should be "seeing the state as an adversary that must be
confronted in a manner similar to industry" (530). Pulido et al. (2016)
argue that EJ movements should be "challenging capitalism or the state's
efforts to protect it" (15), "refusing to participate in regulatory
charades," "oppositional," "challenging the state," and "raising hell
for both polluters and agencies that protect them" (15, 26, 27). Pellow
occasionally offers tempered acknowledgments that engaging with the
state can be part of a productive EJ movement strategy (2018: 14, 24,
52, 151).

However, notwithstanding these complexities within and across their
publications, Pellow, [Pulido, and colleagues are also so dismissive of
the state that it is unclear how even confronting the state would be
anything but futile]{.underline}. Notably, Pulido et al. (2016) insist
through the very end of the article that the state refuses to and/or is
unable to enforce existing laws precisely because "vulnerable
communities, in this case, communities of color, are essential to the
functioning of racial capitalism" and marvel that EJ activists "seem to
believe that the state will actually protect them" (26). Pellow's
writing conveys that engaging with the state is not only futile but
perpetuates injustice. Despite occasionally conceding that, for
instance, "\[s\]ocial movements can strategically harness the power of
states to produce positive outcomes" (2018: 151), such comments are
overshadowed by his insistence that any engagement with the state
legitimizes its inherent violence. Pellow bases this argument on the
state's longstanding history of violence against people of color and
other marginalized groups, as well as his assertion that states'
"pro-socioecological" functions ("ecological protection, environmental
justice, \[and\] civil and human rights") and its "antisocioecological"
functions "tend to be integrally linked in a fashion that serves to
reinforce state power and various forms of inequality" (Pellow, 2018:
23, 57). He argues, "repression and violence are not practices that
states engage in on an occasional basis; they are at the core of what
states do and why they exist \... They are \... inherently authoritarian
and exclusionary forces" (2018: 57--58). As a result, he insists that EJ
movements should pursue change "beyond the state" because anything else
legitimizes these fundamentally violent institutions. His
recommendations align with a broader body of anarchist-inspired
(autonomist) Marxist scholarship that conceptualizes the state as
anathema to justice (see Angel, 2017: 559--560). Pellow writes, "EJ
scholars and activists would be well served by always considering the
pros and cons of embracing the state, and asking how their conceptual
and tactical choices could instead be focused on the most robustly
democratic options" (2018: 24). He implores EJ scholars and activists to
"go beyond the state to find other sources of such support that deepen
and improve the health of our relationships among one another and with
our ecological life support systems" (2021: 60--61).

Within, against, and beyond the state

The arguments by Pellow, Pulido, and colleagues about EJ activists' and
scholars' priorities described above draw important attention to the
state's structural perpetuation of violence and its targeting of the
bodies, families, communities, and lands of Indigenous peoples, people
of color, and other groups. By situating the specifics of EJ policy
implementation into broad historical, structural context, these scholars
compellingly argue that EJ movements need to stridently challenge state
institutions and foster direct democracy beyond the state. That said, I
argue that, despite the complexities of their arguments, they represent
the state as inevitably and fully serving the interests of capital
through racial capitalism and settler colonialism, and that doing so
leads them to problematic recommendations that dismiss the ways in which
states also serve other ends, can be made to do so more meaningfully,
must be made to do so, and are being made to do so.

Notwithstanding the state's many failures to date, government agencies'
modest responses to EJ and other environmental movements nonetheless
have material benefits for communities who cannot otherwise escape from
environmental hazards (Harrison, 2019; Holifield, 2018; King, 2012;
Méndez, 2020; Wilson et al., 2007). The state enacts and facilitates
dispossession, exploitation, and other forms of violence, but also
health clinics, roads, and schools. Its policing apparatus includes
racist surveillance, apprehension, and detention systems, but also
environmental law enforcement that limits air and water pollution. As
countless studies have shown, such institutions and protections are
highly unevenly extended and otherwise insufficient and problematic. But
a critical attention to the workings of state power need not preclude
inquiry into the ways state institutions help reduce harm and meet other
requirements of justice and can be made to do so more meaningfully.

In recent years, [[political ecologists]{.mark} and other geographers
have [marshaled **relational conceptions** **of the state** to challenge
scholarship]{.mark} [that treats the state as **monolithic**]{.mark},
**[ontologically coherent]{.mark}**,]{.underline} [[rigid]{.mark}, [and
entirely repressive]{.mark}]{.underline} (Angel, 2017; Angel and Loftus,
2019; Bakker, 2007; Carroll, 2015; Loftus, 2020; Martin and Pierce,
2012; Robertson, 2010; Routledge et al., 2018; Thelen et al., 2014;
Wright, 2010). [These scholars critique structuralist accounts that
focus exclusively on the coercive functions of the state]{.underline},
calling for greater attention to the variegations, contradictions, and
inconsistencies in state practice. As Davina Cooper (2017: 337--338)
describes,

[Alongside those who see the only "good" states as]{.underline} workers'
states, or states in the process of "[withering away," are
[those]{.mark} who [find transformative potential]{.mark} even
[in]{.mark} the depths of [liberal capitalist states]{.mark} as [they
**uncover contradictions**]{.mark}, [inconsistencies and **plurality in

rationalities]{.mark}**]{.underline} (e.g. Chappell, 2000; Martin and
Pierce, 2012; Newman, 2012; Newman and Clarke, 2014). [[Capitalist
liberal states]{.underline}]{.mark} may reproduce dominant social

[welfare]{.mark}, steward resources, establish [fora for]{.mark} public
[debate]{.mark}, make new critical forms of knowledge

Scholars call for relational and processual theorizations that treat
state institutions as reflecting broader social contestation within
which they are embedded (e.g. Jessop, 2002). As Paul Routledge, Andrew
Cumbers, and Kate Derickson (2018) argue, "[To understand [the **state
as a process**]{.mark} is to [recognize]{.mark} that [it is
constantly]{.mark} being [reworked]{.mark} and remade in ways that shift
the balance of power across institutions and scales]{.underline} with
evolving implications for justice-oriented political responses \... By
viewing the state in terms of an ensemble of institutional structures
and practices, through which particular social groups attempt to develop
their own politics and strategies, we can be attentive to the openings
for progressive political mobilisations" (79, 80). That is,
conceptualizing states as shaped by and reflective of struggle and
contestation positions them as able to be remade. [Rather than reducing
[the state]{.mark} to inherently, inevitably, and wholly oppressive, it
[is better understood as]{.mark} a **[terrain of
struggle]{.mark}**]{.underline} and thus a complex patchwork of
practices that cut in multiple directions. Moreover, [[it is]{.mark}
also [a **necessary site**]{.mark} **of struggle** both [because it is a
**source of harms**]{.mark} [and a **site for** ]{.mark}**helping [to
redress them]{.mark}**]{.underline}[.]{.mark}

Pauline McGuirk and Phillip O'Neill (2012: 1377--1378) explain that
recognizing the functional and relational complexity within
states---viewing them as peopled sites of contestation---highlights
opportunities for political change:

While they \[states\] can appear as actors with a more-than-provisional
identity and coherency, such subjectivity belies their existence as
fields of multi-vocal and multi-scalar, cross-cutting and conflicting
interests \... \[[States are\] resolutely hybrid and multilayered,
resistant to a common, singular rationality, driven by diverse
ideologies]{.underline}, and holding on to multiple political projects
and motivations mobilised simultaneously \... States are always in play,
then, but are subject to reconstruction by the changing discourses,
technologies and practices of governing \... Notwithstanding
neoliberalism's reworking of states' operations, then, state capacity
for progressive social and redistributive interventions remains; and the
practices, technologies and texts which mobilise progressive
interventions are always available to be amplified.

Malini Ranganathan (2016) urges EJ scholars to specify how states
produce and reproduce racist orders and also how they can be used and
reformed toward more environmentally just ends---to identify ways in
which "liberalism's contradictions and ambivalences provide openings for
counter-hegemonic struggle": "[[radical EJ]{.mark} movements [could be
deepened]{.mark} via an [explicit **unmasking of racial
liberalism**]{.mark} **[while]{.mark} also accepting** and
**[strengthening]{.mark} [liberalism's potential]{.mark}** [for
**fighting**]{.mark} **structural [environmental
discrimination]{.mark}**]{.underline}" (29--30).

In their arguments about what EJ activists and scholars do and should
do, Pellow, Pulido, and colleagues dismiss this complexity of states,
instead conceptualizing them as wholly repressive. This leads them to
problematic conclusions. First, Pellow's wholesale condemnation of the
state and constant embrace of scholarship that regards states as
"inherently authoritarian and exclusionary" (58) leaves him emphasizing
that EJ movements should turn away from the state and pursue justice
beyond it. However, [**[turning]{.mark} away [from the state]{.mark}**
[leaves the state **open to**]{.mark} **[further capture by
industry]{.mark}** [and]{.mark} **fuels [neoliberal rollback]{.mark}**
[that exacerbates]{.mark} environmental [injustice]{.mark}]{.underline}
(Faber, 2008; Harrison, 2011; Harvey, 2013; Mascarenhas, 2012). Also,
[turning away from the state leaves intact its structures of
dispossession and violence]{.underline}. Hence the importance of social
movements that challenge the state, including prison abolitionist
movements that engage the state not necessarily as a source of justice
but to reduce some harm it causes (Gilmore, 2017).

Additionally, Pellow's [[examples]{.mark} [of **direct
democracy**]{.mark} [are **very brief and highly
localized**]{.mark}**,**]{.underline} leaving the reader wondering [[how

[**necessary** **spatial and temporal scales**]{.mark}, [such as

### 2AC---Progress Possible

#### Quality of life for arctic indigenous people has risen; disproves necropolitics -- prefer statistics. 

**Poppel 17** (Birger Poppel, researcher of at Department of Culture and
Social History at the University of Greeland; 17; "Well-Being of
Circumpolar Arctic Peoples: The Quest for Continuity", : Estes, R.,
Sirgy, M. (eds) The Pursuit of Human Well-Being. International Handbooks
of Quality-of-Life. Springer, Cham.
https://doi.org/10.1007/978-3-319-39101-4_17) -- Langham EY

[The livelihood and living conditions of the indigenous peoples of the
Arctic have changed rapidly since World War II]{.underline}---the period
in history that has seen the largest immigration of newcomers.
Characterizing the development of the changes in the Arctic as "rapid"
is not a cliché. [**The indigenous peoples have**, generally speaking,
**proven to be both resilient and adaptive despite hardships** due to
injustices arising from a lack of social and cultural empathy from
colonial powers]{.underline}. In many regions, and through the different
arrangements they negotiated with the states they found themselves to be
citizens of, [indigenous peoples have managed largely to maintain
critically important aspects of their lifestyles, including close
relations to nature through subsistence use of the land water and their
living resources.]{.underline} Many indigenous peoples have also
succeeded in retaining their ancestral languages.

The indigenous people make up roughly 10 % of the total Arctic
population. A majority of the Arctic population are, from a historical
perspective, newcomers. All Arctic regions depend on exploitation of
natural resources (renewable and/ or nonrenewable), and all are impacted
by globalization and climate change. These impacts have resulted in
multifaceted challenges because the changes are intertwined with
demographic, socioeconomic, and sociocultural changes.

A key question, therefore, is how the changes have impacted the living
conditions and wellbeing of the indigenous and other residents of the
Arctic. A precondition for being able to answer this question is
developing relevant indicators to measure and compare human development.
Attempts to apply the conventional indicators of the HDI met with many
obstacles because of the lack of comparable official statistics for the
different Arctic jurisdictions. Furthermore, assessing individual
well-being and quality of life, as well as monitoring indicators
reflecting what is most important to indigenous and other Arctic
residents such as ties to nature, cultural continuity and vitality, and
control of destiny, is an even bigger challenge.

That being said, [**the more intense international focus on the
Arctic**, the **increased self-awareness of indigenous peoples**, and
their **creation of national and international organizations** as well
as the establishment of international collaborative entities such as the
Arctic Council **has resulted in a number of promising initiatives
focusing on human development in the circumpolar North**: the AHDR, the
Arctic Social Indicators, and the SLiCA.]{.underline}

The overall **[findings]{.underline}**, which are **[based on official
statistics]{.underline}**, **[indicate that]{.underline}**, generally
speaking, [**conditions have improved** during the first decade of the
twenty-first century **in physical health**]{.underline} ([measured by
infant mortality rate and years of life expectancy]{.underline}),
[**educational** **attainment**]{.underline} (measured by people with a
tertiary education), [**and** **income**]{.underline} (measured by GRP
per capita). The most recent figures and the actual rankings vary among
the different indicators, but [in recent decades **all Arctic regions
have experienced improvements** in physical health, education, and
economic well-being as measured by the HDI indicators.]{.underline} In
comparison, the overall mental health status of many indigenous
populations has suffered.

There are, however, huge differences between regions and between
population groups (especially indigenous and nonindigenous) within
regions. One expression of increased social suffering is the rapid rise
in the rate of death by suicide in several Arctic indigenous
populations, especially among young people.

Surveys comparing well-being among indigenous and nonindigenous groups
in Alaska and Arctic Canada confirm that nonindigenous residents rate
their individual well-being higher than indigenous residents do. [The
SLiCA focused on quality of life of Inuit, Sámi, and the indigenous
peoples of Chukotka and the Kola Peninsula and concluded that despite
facing social problems the majority of Inuit and Sámi were satisfied or
very satisfied with the quality of life in their community.]{.underline}
Among the indigenous people in the two Russian survey regions, the Kola
Peninsula and Chukotka, however, fewer than two out of ten were
satisfied with the quality of life in their community. Furthermore,
[**more than nine out of ten Inuit surveyed** in Greenland and Northern
Alaska, and also Swedish Sámi, **reported that they were satisfied** or
very satisfied **with their quality of life** as a whole.]{.underline}
The availability of fish and wildlife as well as the availability of
wage jobs were important explanatory factors, indicating that to these
indigenous residents a mixed lifestyle is still of utmost importance

### 2AC---Biopolitics Bad

#### Theorizations of biopolitics are insufficient and cedes valuable ground that can actualize material change. Institutions can be malleable, but the K makes it impossible. 

Alexandro **Kioupkiolis 13** is an Associate Professor of Contemporary
Political Theory at Aristotle University, 10/28/2013, "Towards a Regime
of Post-political Biopower? Dispatches from Greece, 2010--2012",
*Theory, Culture & Society*,
https://journals-sagepub-com.proxy.lib.umich.edu/
doi/full/10.1177/0263276413501705, MH

[The concepts of post-politics and biopower may be helpful in theorizing
the current mutations of neoliberal power but they are not fully up to
the task. They have been rightly castigated for constructing an overly
rigid and all-encompassing regime of power which lies beyond challenge
and change.]{.underline} As Bonnie Honig (2009: xv, 66--7, 88) makes
clear, the apocalyptic image of the state of emergency and biopower in
Agamben rules out the possibility of democratic response and cements
'emergency's closures'. It [postulates an all-too solid, omnipotent,
top-down sovereignty which takes exception to the law and cannot be
resisted, covering over remaining fissures, opportunities, drives and
invitations to collective mobilization.]{.underline} Modern sovereignty
could be thought, [instead, 'as a contingent formation that might get
relocated or redistributed in contests over whether a state of exception
should be instituted, in what such a state should consist'
(]{.underline}Honig, 2009[: 88)]{.underline}. In contemporary globalized
capitalism, sovereignty appears to be a multipolar circuit of forces,
'displaced from national to international territories and reconstituted
for the benefit of financial capital' (Saskia Sassen in Dean, 2009:
131--2[). People's power in collective action or electoral politics
remains often a variable component in this mobile, complex and uneven
constellation of forces, at least where liberal democracy has not been
fully torn apart]{.underline}.

Likewise, the 'post-political' qualification of biopower has attracted
incisive criticisms. Jodi Dean argues that left-wing laments about the
rise of technocratic expert rule, the declining significance of
democratic politics under the compulsions of the markets, the
disappearance of the 'political' as radical antagonism are apt but
partial descriptions of our state of affairs. [They obfuscate the
surviving sites of politics and politicization and the real energies of
democratic agency when they are taken as full and comprehensive
accounts. In this case, the emphasis on post-politics 'cedes in advance
key terrains of activism and struggle' (]{.underline}Dean, 2009[:
12).]{.underline} Talk of post-[politics fails thus to recognize and to
engage with the terms of political games which are actually being
played, and it stops short of supporting emerging instances of
democratic agency, producing a vicious circle of self-fulfilling
prophecies]{.underline}.

#### Biopolitics is not all-encompassing -- their theory is a form of confirmation bias that looks for a reason to perpetuate itself. 

Morris Davis **Grossberg 15** **-** Morris Davis Distinguished Professor
University of North Carolina at Chapel Hill We All Want to Change the
World, 10/23/2015,
mhttps://www.academia.edu/13048909/We_all_want_to_change_the_world_The_paradox_of_the_U_S_left_A_polemic\_
MH

[There is]{.underline} [also]{.underline} [a strong tendency
in]{.underline} [current]{.underline} [biopolitical research to
assume]{.underline} that these [operations
of]{.underline} [power]{.underline} always succeed, e.g., [always
produce their own forms of subjectivity]{.underline}, but this move
forgets that Foucault was describing apparatuses or technologies that
seek to produce specific effects. He did not assume that these are the
result of conspiracies (since they are often the result of many
different intentions and projects, none of which imagined the particular
apparatus which finally appeared), or that they are inevitably
successful. Moreover, reality is not simply shaped by any one project,
because there are always other projects. Nor is it the result of any
single apparatus of power since there are always multiple apparatuses,
some able to work harmoniously and even to reinforce others (as Foucault
imagined discipline and biopolitics), some able to uncomfortably
co-exist, and some operating against each other. [Every apparatus
engenders its own resistances,]{.underline} limiting its ability to
fully realize the reality it imagines. And different people and
populations may exist in different relations to the apparatus. To put it
differently, [people]{.underline} [often live]{.underline} [with and
within such configurations]{.underline} [of power]{.underline} [in a
variety of ways; there are different forms of consent and
resistance]{.underline} to the demands of power, and to the ways such
practices attempt to produce and fail to produce reality. To take only
one current example, so-called neoliberal forms of governmentality
supposedly demand---and produce---a new entrepreneurial subject, an
individuality that treats its own life as an enterprise, as human
capital. While I don\'t know if this form of subjectivity is new, I am
pretty sure it is only ever partially successful and its successes are
differentially distributed. There are no doubt instances of real
success, but there are more instances of seriously limited success,
cynical consent or even self-conscious avoidance. [It is
these]{.underline} [complexities]{.underline} [that]{.underline} [need
to be analyzed]{.underline}, for they provide the possibilities for
resistance, struggle and transformation. [The tendency to treat biopower
as if it]{.underline} [were new, as if it were successful
and,]{.underline} I might add, increasingly, [as if
it]{.underline} [were]{.underline} [to be
found]{.underline} [everywhere, is]{.underline} at least in part [the
result of a]{.underline} [fetishism of theory:
concepts]{.underline} [become certainties,]{.underline} [demanding to be
used and]{.underline}, in return, [guaranteeing their own truth. If you
go looking for biopower, you will find it.]{.underline} Notions of
biopolitics have been reinforced by further theoretical developments.
Agamben (1998) challenged Foucault\'s theory by both expanding the
historical relevance of biopolitics and contracting its meaning.
According to Agamben, sovereignty (the law) produces biopolitical life
or better, it claims the power to separate the citizen (zoe---a
qualified life, a form of social life) from bare---purely
biological---existence (bios); it claims the power to reduce the citizen
to homo sacer--- bare life---a product and object of power that exists
in a specific and unique relation to the law and society. Homo sacer
exists in a state of exception, an included exclusion, which is the very
mirror image of the sovereign, the one capable of suspending the law in
the name of the law. The state of exception results in the inclusion of
life itself in the juridicalpolitical order by its very exclusion.
Biopower, thus, takes the biopolitical life of citizens into its
political calculation; it is concerned with governing the exigencies and
variations of biological life. While it has a long history ---at least
back to the Greeks for Agamben, it has a different existence in
modernity, becoming explicit and, as it were, normalized, so that it is
no longer seen as that which must be excluded in order to constitute
society, but precisely as the ground of social existence. For Agamben,
the Nazi concentration camp has become the metonym for modern
deployments of bare life, founding a society on the extended duration of
the state of exception. Thus, while Agamben has in one sense a more
specific meaning for biopolitics, he also offers a more general sense of
power asserting itself in and through life as biopolitical production,
and defining the present as a new epoch. The turn to biopolitics more
generally has had important implications for thinking about social
differences and the modalities of exclusion and belonging. It has opened
up new materialist and biological understandings of life, bodies, and
environments as heterogeneous and dynamic. Such analyses, for example,
point to the efforts by various political and capitalist agents to take
up the possibilities of the new molecular and evolutionary biologies to
intervene into the processes of life and the behaviors of bodies, but
the place or relevance of such efforts in relation to larger
organizations of power remains speculative at best. This biological
naturalism also tries to make visible the ways bodies---from corporeal
capacities to the bio-chemical and genetic---serve as material actors or
agents in political relations and struggles. It points to a number of
serious and even unprecedented bio-political and bio-ethical questions,
including: environmental degradation and global climate change, with the
related issues of energy, economic growth and consumer directed
economies; the possibilities of genetic and technological interventions
into life itself; the commodification of aspects of life; the
destruction of genetic diversity, and the transformations of agriculture
and food chains; political, economic and military interventions into
matters of health and well-being; and technological transformations of
the possibilities and relations of sensory experiences of the world.
Again, [one has to ask---and investigate---exactly what is new about
such efforts---after all, efforts to manage and reconfigure bodies and
environments have a longer history in genetics, agriculture, war,
technology, labor (from slavery to Fordism), media and consumption,
etc., although they may not have had the tools to accomplish their task
in such efficient, directed and complicated ways]{.underline}. Many
critics currently working on these issues do recognize such histories,
and often acknowledge as well the important contributions of feminists,
class theorists and critical analysts of race and colonialism. At the
same time, however, this [materialist work often assumes a historical
rupture, that there is \"something unprecedented about our contemporary
situation in which the prefix \'bio-\' proliferates,\" which challenges
\"some of the most basic assumptions that have underpinned the modern
world\" (Coole and Frost, 2010, pp. 15 and 4), that we have entered a
new epoch, constituted by new technologies, practices and forms of
power]{.underline}. It suggests that the new forms of biological and
biotechnical intervention have opened up the possibilities of new forms
of--- micropolitical---control. In Foucault, micropolitics, or capillary
power, describes power operating by moving through and across bodies, or
better, fragments of the body (eyes, hands, etc.) without the mediation
of consciousness and ideology. It is unclear whether Foucault thought
capillary power has always existed, or whether it was introduced with
and made possible the new forms of biopower of the 18th century. But in
either case, there is nothing new about micropolitics today. Perhaps the
current forms of biological rationality and technology have enabled the
development of self-conscious and intentional forms of micropolitical
manipulation, but these have not yet become omnipresent or omnipotent.
[Within theories of biopolitics, everything can become biopolitical; to
offer just one example, Hardt and Negri (2011, pp. 58-9) use it to
describe the practices of immaterial labor as \"the localized productive
powers of life -- that is, the production of affects and languages
through social cooperation and the interaction of bodies and desires,
the invention of new forms of the relation to the self and others, and
so forth.\" Apparently, the biopolitical can be eerily
immaterial]{.underline}. And I am thrown back to the old pragmatic
maxim: [a difference that makes no difference is no
difference.]{.underline} [If everything is
biopolitical]{.underline}---and in one obvious sense it
is, [then]{.underline} [it]{.underline} **[adds very
little]{.underline}** after its first utterance. But such descriptions
of the contemporary conjuncture leave many questions unanswered and even
unaddressed: [Is there anything---any form of activity---that is not
biopolitical? If all of human life]{.underline} (including, e.g.,
labor) [is]{.underline} biopolitical, [hasn\'t it always
been?]{.underline} And if it has, then what is old and what is new? What
are the consequences---the successes and the failures---of the sorts of
relations and arrangements of power it attempts to map? [How does
biopower connect with other forms of power, including those that its
champions (on both sides as it were) seem to deny too quickly---forms of
power involving cognition, consciousness, signification and
representation]{.underline}?

#### Biopolitics confines itself to a mode of knowledge production that justifies inaction. You should reject the method as Left-wing pampering. 

**Solis & Moya 20**, Angel Octavio Alvarez Solis is professor of
philosophy at the university Iberoamericana in Mexico City. Moya holds
an M.A. in philosophy from the same university. "Against Biopolitics:
Compensation and Confinement: Entombing the Academic Left", 9/25/2020,
https://publicseminar.org/essays/against-biopolitics/, MH

[Perhaps due to its successful discursive dissemination, there are few
thinkers --the less judicious or the most reactionary ones-- who
have questioned Michel Foucault's late ideas. These disputes about the
"truth" of biopolitics culminate in such paroxysm that critique has
become a continuous theological diatribe. Everyone is engaged
in an amendment or a commentary on the grand biopolitical text: Paul
B. Preciado disputes Roberto Esposito; Esposito corrects Jean-Luc Nancy; Nancy revises Giorgio Agamben; Agamben adjusts Foucualt, and
so on, until any of us --any average reader-- finds oneself accepting
that the virus is a product of biopower.]{.underline} However, if we
pause the time of the event, [if we take a step back from the totalizing
demand of biopolitics, we may envisage that the biopolitical vocabulary
disables any outside and all exits.]{.underline}

[As a good compensatory theory, biopolitics is an anthropological
diagnosis. As a good theology, biopolitics substantiates life and
kidnaps its own subject matter: life, whether it is one's own, the life
of others, or life in its constitutive fragility.]{.underline}

I am probably exaggerating and not all biopolitics amounts to
confinement. The demand for confinement is probably compatible with
ungovernable life. Indeed, maybe the intensification of absolute control
over life is just beginning to constitute a new world order. Every
crisis has its own doubts, exaggerations, and prophecies. But crises are
also measures of time, indicators, and thresholds. The pandemic is
undoubtedly a threshold. A threshold that doesn't open up a future
because the future is the mode of time currently in jeopardy, so we have
no options or decisions to make about it. [Therefore, the problem is
that if biopolitics is defined as the sovereign administration of life,
it is precisely because, ultimately, life cannot be administered. The
sovereignty of the body is a liberal illusion. Life is always
ungovernable. As Ariadne's thread, biopolitics weaves its sovereign
arguments to construct the garment of confinement.]{.underline}

[Due to the latter, biopolitics sets up subjectivity in a permanent
state of war: a subjectivity articulated as a phármakon that is capable
of carrying within it both evil and salvation.]{.underline} A gnostic
subjectivity. A subjectivity that cures and poisons. A subjectivity with
friends and enemies. [A subjectivity which is at war with itself and
with the world because its isolation "saves lives" and, even if
"contaminated," has no other choice but survival.]{.underline} That the
possibility of a virus lives within each of us implies that everyone
bears the accelerated death of others and oneself. Biopolitics
transforms us into Poe's red masque or into Melville's Bartleby: forms
of anguish that cover the death drive. Biopolitics is always
necropolitics.

Biopolitics occludes the right to death. Biopolitics ensures that life
--just as death-- is a political act, that burying our dead is a
political activity and, in such a total capture of existence, we find
one of the greatest obstacles to live again. Not even the dead are safe
from the exercises of sovereignty. The dead deserve to be outside
statistical expropriation. This is why Giambattista Vico, the
philosopher of the Italian plague of 1656, was not wrong when he claimed
that, no matter how barbaric or civilized, every human form of life
possesses religion, marriage and burial. The loss of the burial rite is
the victory of biopolitics. The number trumps the name. The right to
bury our dead is the first and last locus of resistance against
biopolitical closure. The tomb is the last testimony of life, of our
life. [Therefore, biopolitics cannot be used to exit biopolitics. We
must avoid the biopolitical temptation of Antigone's oblivion because,
as María Zambrano reminds us, "Antigone's tomb is our own obscured
consciousness."]{.underline}

[Biopolitics forecloses the possibility of learning to live amongst
specters, amongst the "non-living," amongst the "dead" or the "nor
living nor dead,"]{.underline} because its aim of governing all life
penetrates the fantasy that life is more important than death: the death
of anyone, the death of our beloved, the death of the
unknown. [Biopolitics is not interested in tombs but in morgues, it
does not imagine cemeteries but death pits, it does not project lives
but counts deaths.]{.underline} Finally, [as
a psychic structure, biopolitics is not capable of governing life nor
generating a sense for survival.]{.underline} As a theory, [biopolitics
does not explain how the life of the dead is possible, nor does it
consider the importance of burial for life itself.]{.underline}
Maybe [it can only envision the politics of death as a statistical
extension of populations. Biopolitics does not conceive of an outside
where not all life is political and not all death is a political
issue. Biopolitics is, ultimately, academia's mode of confinement. For
this reason, slightly fragile and untimely, biopolitics is the
compensation of the academic Left in order to avoid contemplating its
own tomb and realizing that, for a long time, it has carried the virus
of sad passions within itself.]{.underline}

### 2AC---Pessimism Bad

#### Their cynicism is bad -- hegemonic systems can change only without nihilism 

**Bloom 8.** Peter Bloom is currently a permanent lecturer in economics
and politics at Swansea University. At the time of this writing, Peter
has received his MA from the University of Essex and was instructing at
that institution. Peter received a doctorate in politics and philosophy
at the University of Essex and also has taught at Cambridge University,
Swansea University, and for a year and Jiangsu University in China.
"Capitalism\'s Cynical Leviathan: Cynicism, Totalitarianism, and Hobbes

#CutWithKirby -
http://zizekstudies.org/index.php/ijzs/article/viewFile/95/161

Yet such [[cynicism **empirically**]{.underline}]{.mark} [[has not
served as a flashpoint for]{.mark} more [transformatory
changes.]{.mark}]{.underline} Both Knights and McCabe as well as Wanous,
Richter, and Austin note the ultimately non-revolutionary character of
this cynicism. Fleming and Spicer (2003) have accordingly sought to
theoretically explain this disjuncture between internal dissatisfaction
and continued external compliance with organizational desires. Their
essay \"Working at a Cynical Distance\" illuminates the relationship
between cynicism and capitalist conformity. Drawing on the work of Lacan
and Zizek they reveal how [[cynical attitudes **reinforce organizational
obedience**]{.underline}]{.mark} [[through displacing resistance **away
from**]{.mark} **actual [practical change]{.mark}**]{.underline}
[[and]{.mark} **[towards]{.mark}**]{.underline} a
**[[complacency]{.underline}]{.mark}** of internal disagreement. Here,
[[cynicism acts as a salve]{.underline}]{.mark} for individuals who
realize the futility of their working experience yet refuse or are
unable to actualize this dissent. [[Consequently]{.underline}]{.mark},
the very presence of subjective [[distancing works as a barrier ***to
more effective campaigns of resistance*.**]{.mark}]{.underline} How are
we then to understand this seeming contradiction between the daily
presence of individual subjective dis-identification and a continued
compliance to capitalist organizational prerogatives? This cynical
totalitarianism speaks theoretically to two competing elements integral
to contemporary capitalist regulation-namely hegemony and the fetishist
disavowal. Far from being separate both play into and enhance the
overall strength of the other. Interrogating the dominant theorists of
each perspective, Laclau and Zizek respectively, speaks to their
ultimate compatibility. Laclau\'s notion of
[[hegemony]{.underline}]{.mark} highlights how an organizational
ideology [[can]{.underline}]{.mark} suture itself as an all-encompassing
subjectivity due to the fact that discourses [ontologically]{.underline}
[[seek to dominate]{.underline}]{.mark} totally [a given]{.underline}
social [space.]{.underline} [[However]{.underline}]{.mark} [[implicit in
the]{.underline}]{.mark} Laclauian [[account is the **eternal
availability**]{.underline}]{.mark} [[of]{.mark} ***[contestation to
this hegemon]{.mark}***]{.underline}ic discourse as no one subjectivity
can completely monopolize a subject\'s understanding. [It is at this
juncture between]{.underline} complete interpellation and [hegemony that
cynicism reveals its importance]{.underline} for individual inscription,
an idea expressed most clearly in the work of Zizek. The inherently
incomplete nature of this hegemony provides the very terrain for a
cynical obedience-as one can recognize the inadequacies of a hegemonic
discourse without thinking beyond its ideological horizons. At stake
thus is not internal coherence of a hegemonic discourse but the forms of
enjoyment it provides to its inscribed subject. To this end an
individual is able to participate within a hegemonic field of meaning
without internally accepting its over-arching truth value. Put
differently, [[a dominant social understanding is sustained **exactly
through**]{.underline}]{.mark} the allowance of internal subjective
disagreement premised on [[the **perceived inability to change
prevailing systems of power**.]{.underline}]{.mark}

### 2AC---Markets Good

#### Markets and capital accumulation can [advance Indigenous emancipation]{.underline}.

**Henry, et al, 17**---Te Ara Poutama, Auckland University of Technology

[Indigenous peoples practise traditions **unique** to their

peoples have highlighted the **negative impacts** of]{.underline} those
[majority cultures]{.underline}, most notably [through **conquest** and
**colonisation**]{.underline}. [[The ability of Indigenous peoples to
**survive** and **flourish**]{.underline},]{.mark} [despite these
impacts]{.underline}, [often [rests on the **enterprises** they create
to achieve their goals for **social change** and **social
justice**]{.mark}]{.underline}.

[**Indigenous entrepreneurship** is a foundation for understanding
**Indigenous enterprise**]{.underline}, for it is often [Indigenous
entrepreneurs]{.underline} that [create and operate such
entities]{.underline}. [Indigenous entrepreneurship has been defined
as]{.underline}, '[the **[creation]{.mark}**]{.underline}[,
**[management]{.underline}**, [and **development** of new ventures by
Indigenous people for the benefit of Indigenous
people]{.underline}]{.mark}. The organisations thus created can pertain
to either the private, public or non-profit sectors' (Hindle & Moroz,
2010: 363). Hindle and Moroz developed a framework for [Indigenous
entrepreneurship]{.underline} research that [[highlights the]{.mark}
relative [importance of **communities**]{.mark} rather than
**individuals**]{.underline}, [and]{.underline} which [[emphasises
**culture** and **social norms** over **resources** and
**profits**]{.underline}]{.mark}.

Peredo, Anderson, Gailbraith, Honig, and Dana (2004) acknowledge that
[[Indigenous peoples strive to **plan** and **control** their own
development]{.underline}]{.mark}, [their **[rights over
resources]{.mark}**]{.underline}, [and to **[rebuild their
communities]{.mark}**]{.underline}. However, Indigenous development
founded on Indigenous culture and society has proved problematic, as
[Indigenous entities [**struggle to exist** in the economic]{.mark} and
organisational [systems of the dominant culture]{.mark}]{.underline}. In
a similar vein, Peredo and McLean (2010) discuss how [Indigenous peoples
around the world have turned to **entrepreneurial endeavour** to improve
the conditions of themselves and their communities]{.underline}. They
suggest that '[Indigenous [communities act **collectively** as]{.mark}
both [**entrepreneur** and **enterprise**]{.mark}]{.underline}[, [with
profit-making]{.underline}]{.mark} [**[subordinate]{.mark}** to social
outcomes]{.underline}' (2010: 614).

For Cahn, '[In Indigenous societies **throughout the
world**]{.underline} "**[business]{.underline}**" [and **economic
activities** are embedded in **cultural** and **social
aspects**]{.underline}, [creating **unique styles** of
entrepreneurship]{.underline}, which are [often
**community-oriented**]{.underline}, and [with **diverse livelihood
outcomes**]{.underline}' (2008: 1). Her focus is Indigenous
entrepreneurship, culture and micro-enterprise in Samoa. Cahn found
that, 'where micro-enterprises blend well with fa'aSamoa1 , an
"Indigenous" style of enterprise develops, and fa'aSamoa is a motivating
factor and asset, which enhances entrepreneurial activity' (2008: 16).
Further, she found that [whilst cash was an **important outcome** of
enterprise]{.underline}, [so too was]{.underline} the [**social** and
**cultural capital**]{.underline}, 'that was [developed through
**actively engaging** in]{.underline} the [values and
expression]{.underline} of fa'aSamoa' (2008: 17).

In their definitive text Dana and Anderson note that, 'there is a rich
heterogeneity among Indigenous peoples, and some of their cultural
values are often incompatible with the basic assumptions of mainstream
theories. Indigenous entrepreneurship often has non-economic explanatory
variables... We propose that entrepreneurship opportunity recognition
and evaluation is therefore culturally determined: however, we note that
culturally determined opportunities for entrepreneurship are often
disrupted by entities external to Indigenous peoples' (2007: 601).

Further, Henry, Dana, and Murphy (2017: 5) argue that [[Indigenous
entrepreneurship]{.underline}]{.mark} differs from the dominant
theoretic themes of mainstream research into entrepreneurship, which
tends to focus on the characteristics of individuals. The literature
from Indigenous scholars, on the other hand, often [[focusses on the
**emancipatory nature** of **entrepreneurial actions**]{.mark} and
**intent**]{.underline}. They write, 'The approach is different because
it focusses on how [entrepreneurs and communities can **surmount [unique
social]{.mark}** [and **environmental constraints**]{.mark}]{.underline}
that apply to them'.

Herein lies one of the conceptual overlaps between social innovation,
cultural capital, and Indigenous entrepreneurship. Aramburu and Saenz
(2011) note that an organisation's ability to innovate is linked to its
intellectual capital, or new knowledge. This paper argues that an
organisation's innovation capabilities are also linked to its cultural
capital, which Throsby defines as 'an asset embodying cultural value'
(1999: 3). Throsby adds that culture should be added to the three
traditionally acknowledged forms of capital -- physical, human, and
natural -- because it enriches economic analyses. The [Indigenous
entrepreneurship]{.underline} literature also [[highlights the
**significance of**]{.underline}]{.mark} both social and cultural
**[[capital]{.underline}]{.mark}**. Light and Dana (2013) recognise that
social [capital **assists** Indigenous entrepreneurs in terms of
the]{.underline} social, networking and others [[**resources** it brings
and the **strategic relationships** it generates]{.underline}]{.mark},
whilst Henry, Dana, and Murphy (2017) link cultural capital to
environmental context, personal identity and self-efficacy, which have
been found to enhance entrepreneurial intent amongst Indigenous peoples.

### 2AC---Turn: Climate Change

#### Climate change poses a threat to Arctic indigenous peoples -- turns the K. 

**Mardikian & Galani 23** (Lisa Mardikian, Senior Lecturer in Property
Law at Brunel University London; Sofia Galani, Assistant Professor of
International Law at the Panteio University of Athens and a Scientific
Advisor to the Scientific Council of the Hellenic Parliament; 8-7-23
"Protecting the Arctic Indigenous Peoples' Livelihoods in the Face of
Climate Change: The Potential of Regional Human Rights Law and the Law
of the Sea Open Access", Human Rights Law Review, Volume 23, Issue 3,
September 2023, ngad020; DOI: https://doi.org/10.1093/hrlr/ngad020) --
Langham EY

The Arctic is home to approximately 4 million people and 9 per cent of
them are indigenous peoples who live in settlements across two
continents and eight countries.^19^ Approximately 155,000 Inuit reside
in Alaska, Canada, Greenland and Russia; 55,000 Athabaskans and Gwich'in
people live in Alaska and Canada, and 15,000 Aleut live in different
territories of Alaska and Russia.^20^ Russia is also home to 250,000
members of different indigenous groups, including the Sami people.^21^
The Sami people are the only indigenous people recognised in Europe^22^
and live across territories in Norway, Finland and Sweden.^23^ [The
majority of Arctic settlements are located on permafrost, and nearly
half of them are coastal permafrost settlements, which reinforces the
importance of the distinct Arctic environment to the livelihoods and
cultural identity of the Arctic indigenous peoples.]{.underline}^24^

**[Climate change has had a profound impact on the living environment of
Arctic indigenous peoples]{.underline}**, and thus, it merits special
attention. The Artic is characterised 'as the world's climate change
barometer'.^25^ According to the reports of the Intergovernmental Panel
on Climate Change (IPCC), 'surface warming in the Arctic will continue
to be more pronounced than the global average warming over the 21st
century'.^26^ [It is expected that '**additional warming** will lead to
more frequent and intense marine heatwaves and is projected to further
**amplify permafrost thawing** and loss of seasonal snow cover,
glaciers, land ice and Arctic sea ice'.]{.underline}^27^ A significant
decline in sea ice and glaciers is already being witnessed in the
Chukchi and Beaufort seas.^28^ Decline in seasonal sea ice and higher
sea levels increase coastal hazards, such as flooding and coastal
erosion.^29^ Such [changes have resulted in, and are projected to
further lead to, **restrictions on indigenous peoples' access to
ancestral lands and natural resources, loss of land** and houses or
**forced relocation** and mass resettlements]{.underline}, which
directly affect the enjoyment of the right to property.^30^ In addition,
[there have been serious consequences for the main sources of income of
indigenous communities, as the thinni**ng of ice makes it more difficult
to hunt and fish** and **climate change has led to the degradation of
reindeer pastures** and to a **lack of available food** for reindeer
herding.]{.underline}^31^

[**Ocean warming** and acidification are also responsible for the
**depletion of fish stocks** and decline in other marine species,
**limiting the opportunities of Arctic indigenous people to access
marine resources, threatening their income, food security and cultural
heritage.**]{.underline}^32^ [Reduction in sea ice may facilitate
overfishing, which]{.underline}, in turn, [is likely to affect the fish
stocks and the fishing practices of Arctic indigenous peoples, many of
whom rely on small-scale subsistence fishing.]{.underline}^33^
[Sea-level rise]{.underline} can also change coastal configuration or
lead to the partial or complete loss of islands. This will affect the
baselines from which maritime zones are measured and may result in their
reduction or disappearance. ^34^ Such scenario [could severely limit the
access of the Arctic indigenous communities to natural resources,
including their fishing rights.]{.underline}

Beyond the direct impact that rising temperatures have on the enjoyment
of the living environment of the indigenous communities, it is worth
evaluating the indirect impact that climate change has had on it. [The
**receding ice cap has made the waters more navigable.** **Enhanced
accessibility** has been as**sociated with a growth in all forms of
shipping.**]{.underline}^35^ [**Ship operations** are **associated
with** high risks for the marine environment, such as
**pollution**]{.underline} (oil, air and noise pollution, sewage and
garbage) [and damage or disruption to the marine ecosystem through
ballast water and hull biofouling or by icebreaking and animal deaths
caused by ship strikes.]{.underline}^36^ [**Black carbon emissions from
shipping and oil spills** have been a major cause for alarm because of
the **disastrous impact** they could have on the fragile ecosystem of
the coastal communities.]{.underline} The rapid expansion of extractive
activities has also caused concern to the Arctic indigenous communities.
[**Pipelines**, oil and gas extraction and production are known for
transforming and **polluting the environment in diverse ways forcing the
Arctic indigenous peoples to relocate** or adapt their social and
economic practices to different ways of living, such as by adapting
their harvesting, hunting and fishing practices.]{.underline}^37^
Considering the changes reported in the Arctic environment, which have a
significant impact on the livelihoods of indigenous peoples, the next
section explores the extent to which regional human rights law can
contribute to protecting the right to property against some of the
effects of climate change.

### 2AC---Turn: Russia War

#### Indigenous people foot the cost of Russian warfare -- preventing escalation is key to halting further discrimination and violence.

**Lackenbauer et al. 23** (P. Whitney Lackenbauer, NAADSN Network Lead;
Evgeniia Sidorova, Research Fellow of the School of Public Policy at the

Pro-Kremlin Narratives and Voices of Dissent";

-- Langham EY

In February 2023, [Pavel Sulyandziga highlighted the disproportionate
number of Russian Indigenous people who have been conscripted to fight
in the War in Ukraine. On the Russian side, the number of Indigenous
combatants in the war is proportionally higher than the number of ethnic
Russians combatants. With mobilization criteria paying no respect to
Indigeneity and the roles of men within those societies, recruitment has
had deleterious effects on the cultures and traditions of Indigenous
peoples in Russia.]{.underline}41 Furthermore, [data sets]{.underline}
(collected by a team of volunteers from the social media and other
available sources) [demonstrate large inequalities in the army mortality
rates across Russian regions]{.underline}, with the highest mortality of
soldiers originating from poor regions in Siberia and the Russian Far
East and the lowest from Moscow and St. Petersburg.42 In short, [the
share of Indigenous peoples in the total number of deaths in the war
appears to be disproportionately high, based on official Russian
statistics and numbers provided by Russian scientists.]{.underline}
Consequently, [it is reasonable to conclude that the war in Ukraine has
rendered Russia's Indigenous peoples more vulnerable and has imposed a
disproportionate burden and cost on them.]{.underline} This story is not
well known in Russia owing to censorship.

A 2022 article by Umea University (Sweden) doctoral student Ekaterina
Zmyvalova provides a helpful summary of how the war in Ukraine
negatively affects the human rights of Indigenous small-numbered peoples
of Russia. These include the right to freedom of expression, the right
to enjoy their culture, and the right to life, liberty and security.
Specific impacts include:

> • Russia's growing disregard for its international legal obligations;
>
> • [the introduction of new sanctions against persons and organizations
> who do not support the Russian regime's official discourses about the
> war, and increased pressure on Indigenous peoples' representatives to
> strictly adhere to state messaging or face intimidation]{.underline}
> (like the Tannagasheva case noted in the previous section) [and severe
> legal consequences]{.underline};
>
> • [the active participation of Indigenous small-numbered peoples in
> the war due to socio- economic challenges facing their
> communities]{.underline} (including a lack of wage-earning
> opportunities compared to other Russian populations) [and the absence
> of reliable and objective information about the war in
> Ukraine]{.underline};
>
> • [a decline in international cooperation with the Indigenous
> small-numbered peoples of Russia]{.underline} (as it is hard for
> international organizations to trust Russian-based Indigenous people's
> organizations because of their adherence to the Kremlin's narratives
> about the war); and
>
> • [increasing constraints on Russian Indigenous peoples in
> participating in international decision-making, including the Arctic
> Council]{.underline} (given the decision by the seven like- minded
> Arctic states to suspend participation in Council activities involving
> Russia).43

In a short update released in January 2023, Zmyvalova notes that, [on
the Russian side, the number of Indigenous combatants in the war is
proportionally higher than the number of ethnic Russians
combatants.]{.underline} "[The Indigenous population is more vulnerable
and on average of lower socio- economic status than ethnic
Russians]{.underline}," she notes, "hence participation in the war is
financially more attractive for them." Furthermore, activists note that
mobilizing persons from areas "far from Moscow and St. Petersburg is a
way for the Kremlin to reduce the draft's impact on major cities, where
the chances of opposition protests are higher." Most Russian Indigenous
populations reside in small settlements or villages, some of which have
seen most of the male population mobilized. With mobilization criteria
paying no respect to Indigeneity and the roles of men within those
societies, [recruitment has had deleterious effects on the cultures and
traditions of Indigenous peoples in Russia.]{.underline}44

[Alexey Bessudnov]{.underline}, a sociology professor at the University
of Exeter, [released a paper]{.underline} in December 2022 [showing
ethnic and regional inequalities in mortality in the Russian army during
the 2022 war in Ukraine.]{.underline} Based on a data set (collected by
a team of volunteers from the social media and other available sources)
containing the names of about 9,500 Russian servicemen killed in Ukraine
from February to November 2022, [he demonstrates large inequalities in
the army mortality rates across Russian regions]{.underline}, with the
highest mortality of soldiers originating from poor regions in Siberia
and the Russian Far East and the lowest from Moscow and St. Petersburg.
[Some ethnic minority groups, in particular Buryats]{.underline} (a
Mongolic ethnic group native to southeastern Siberia) [and
Tuvans]{.underline} (a Turkic ethnic group Indigenous to Siberia ) [are
overrepresented among the fatalities compared to their share of the
Russian population.]{.underline} Bessudnov suggests that [ethnic
fatality gaps are largely driven by socio-economic
inequalities]{.underline}, with young men in poorer regions see the
career in the military as more attractive.45 In short, [statistic
evidence confirms that most Russian army recruits and conscripts come
from peripheral regions]{.underline} where unemployment, drug addiction
and criminality are rampant. Furthermore, [the share of Indigenous
peoples in the total number of deaths in the war appears to be
disproportionately high, based on official Russian statistics and
numbers provided by Russian scientists.]{.underline} Consequently, [it
is reasonable to conclude that the war in Ukraine has rendered Russia's
Indigenous peoples more vulnerable and has imposed a disproportionate
burden and cost on them.]{.underline} This story does not appear to be
well known or accepted in Russia.

#### Preventing Russian expansionism/war is key to stopping further rights violations against Arctic indigenous peoples.

**Lackenbauer et al. 23** (P. Whitney Lackenbauer, NAADSN Network Lead;
Evgeniia Sidorova, Research Fellow of the School of Public Policy at the
University of Calgary; Sergey Sukhankin, NAADSN Postdoctoral Fellow;
7-21-23; "RAIPON, Russian Indigenous Peoples, and the War in Ukraine:
Pro-Kremlin Narratives and Voices of Dissent";
https://d1wqtxts1xzle7.cloudfront.net/105373741/2023_RAIPON_Russian_Indigenous_PWL_ES_SS_NAADSN_policy_primer-libre.pdf?1693340902=&response-content-disposition=inline%3B+filename%3DRAIPON_Russian_Indigenous_Peoples_and_th.pdf&Expires=1752701405&Signature=hFAHgD-9-W84X6w-hgOMZ7J284G5asi2hMQoHJdfh8HBUIdQ2St82-K1M4MYSdlwrOIy0DTDRDF-\~H78e-HUJ4LvGVnYa49Z8eC-QXrJxKahaBYQC\~X2WCX9nXOXfbdWAZYrJAC\~yOV5rRS5Z8Hg9TAHlDGd6NmuIW5hJafU\~D6DATw-dIO4HjSpqXwrwKzGF-2-N3oEuzSPjOU7RQ9EZahCmC4vLCFKF3SoK72AKeHTE9VrLqCL1k8-GhBfEDiCtgsm7M8oi3qj4lXgtfyhIwkeTH1ditCWzKX2O6ixTPRIwPDdy8nFP3tfDg4cCnoGOHiPbh6PlPOT27BcIeCxtQ\_\_&Key-Pair-Id=APKAJLOHF5GGSLRBV4ZA)
-- Langham EY

[In the wake of Russia's expanded war of aggression against Ukraine,
Russia's northern Indigenous communities have experienced a
disproportionate degree of hardship.]{.underline} Given their small
sizes, remoteness, and ethnicities, these [communities]{.underline}
[have been]{.underline} particularly [targeted by Russian mobilization
efforts. This builds on decades of Russian state abuse, surrounding land
use and resource development that has ignored local concerns and damaged
traditional Indigenous territories. Arctic Indigenous
leadership]{.underline}, in the form of the Russian Association of
Indigenous Peoples of the North (RAIPON) [has been coopted by the
Kremlin and is now little more than a mouthpiece for government
directives.]{.underline} Consequently, [many Indigenous leaders have
fled Russia]{.underline} -- but these political exiles retain important
networks inside Russia, where locals continue to resist state
oppression. Nevertheless, [while Russia has long paid lip service to
Indigenous rights, the state's growing totalitarian tendencies and
confrontation with the West has led to near total state control over
regional representative organizations.]{.underline}

This NAADSN Policy Primer begins with a background overview of RAIPON
and its transformation into a state-controlled organ in the early 2010s
before analyzing its statements supporting Russia's war of aggression
against Ukraine. Our analysis discerns that it has promoted three key
narratives since February 2022: that RAIPON is a staunch supporter of
the Kremlin and of President Vladimir Putin personally; that RAIPON is a
strategic link between Russia and the so-called "newly acquired
territories" (Donetsk, Luhansk, Kherson and Zaporizhian oblasts); and
that RAIPON is the only legitimate representative of Russia's Arctic and
Siberian Indigenous population. We then provide a short assessment of
how [the War in Ukraine has affected Russia's Indigenous peoples,
despite Russian narratives claiming widespread Indigenous support for
the war effort.]{.underline} The last section identifies voices of
dissent associated with the International Committee of Indigenous
Peoples of Russia (ICIPR) and the "Indigenous Russia" (iRussia)
information center. Our conclusions reflect on how political divisions
amongst the Arctic states, dramatically exacerbated by the War in the
Ukraine, are also reflected amongst and within the Permanent
Participants to the Arctic Council.

### 2AC---Refusal Fails

#### Refusal fails.

**Shulman,** **21**---teaches political theory at The Gallatin School of
New York University (George, "Fred Moten's Refusals and Consents: The
Politics of Fugitivity," Political Theory, Vol. 49(2), 272--313, dml)
\[inserted "when" for grammatical integrity---insertion denoted by
brackets\]

In turn, radical democrats may refuse his reduction of politics to
sovereignty, but [if we]{.underline} then [identify the]{.underline}
**[properly political]{.underline}** [as]{.underline}
**[[nonsovereign]{.underline}]{.mark}** action, as nonrule or (fugitive)
**[[refusal]{.underline}]{.mark}** to be governed, [[we]{.underline}
[remain]{.underline} **[captive]{.underline}** [to]{.underline}]{.mark}
[this]{.underline} **[demonic picture]{.underline}** [of
[power]{.mark}]{.underline} [[and its]{.underline} [**idealized
other**.]{.underline}]{.mark} [By affirming]{.underline}
**[only]{.underline}** [the "**power to**" of solidarity and action in
concert, [we]{.mark}]{.underline} [[risk]{.underline}
**[disavowing]{.underline}** [power "**over**," as]{.underline}
**[participation]{.underline}** **[in rule]{.underline}**]{.mark}[,

[and]{.underline} [as "**ruling out**" antidemocratic
interests]{.underline} [and practices.]{.underline} [Do we imagine that
generativity thrives]{.underline} **[only by refusing]{.underline}**
[**rule**,]{.underline} [and not also through]{.underline} [forms
of]{.underline} **[structure]{.underline}** [and even]{.underline}
**[imposition]{.underline}**, as parents and teachers know? As Prospero,
a personification of both sovereignty and theory, finally acknowledged

[between subjection to sovereign rule as such, or statelessness
as]{.underline} **[refusal to be governed]{.underline}** [as such, and
"come down to earth"]{.underline} as Marx put it? [[We then]{.underline}
[find politicality]{.underline} **[not in rule]{.underline}**
[or]{.underline} **[nonrule]{.underline}**]{.mark}, as such,
[but]{.underline} [in the]{.underline} [**[judgments]{.underline}**
[and]{.underline} **[actions]{.underline}**]{.mark} [by]{.underline}
[which]{.underline} [subalterns]{.underline} [address]{.underline}
**[who makes decisions]{.underline}** [(and]{.underline} [**how**)

and]{.underline} **[inequalities]{.underline}** [are]{.underline}
